# File Explanation

This is meant to supplement existing China files. Since much of the
internal link / impact work was done by other labs, this file focused on
a new link to China SOI. The plan strengthens US presence (predominately
commercial) in the Arctic, which is bad because it shifts our presence
away from Panama. China fill-in displaces or trades off directly with US
presence -- the impacts include leadership/hegemony/democracy. We tried
to organize some of the relevant work from other files at the bottom for
convenience.

# DA \-- Tradeoff \-- FWZ

## 1nc

### 1nc -- Readiness

#### Trump is [cracking down]{.underline} on Chinese influence now\-\--that prevents [dual-use]{.underline} and rapid militarization of the Canal.

**Curley '25** \[Gregg; 1/9/25; Senior US Marine Corps Fellow, JD from
Roger Williams University, MA in Military Operational Art and Science
from the Air Command and Staff College, MA in Military Studies from the
Marine Corps Command and Staff College; Atlantic Council, "The US is
right to be concerned about China's influence over the Panama Canal,"
https://www.atlanticcouncil.org/blogs/new-atlanticist/right-to-be-concerned-about-chinas-influence-over-the-panama-canal/\]

President-elect Donald [Trump's]{.underline} recent [rhetoric
on]{.underline} territorial ambitions in the Western
Hemisphere---ranging from [retaking the Panama
Canal](https://www.msn.com/en-au/news/other/trump-threatens-to-retake-panama-canal-in-truth-social-post/ar-AA1wir1S?ocid=BingNewsSerp) and [buying
Greenland](https://apnews.com/article/trump-greenland-norway-panama-canal-canada-a52858e3075f9b5ad95e78753293fc1f) to [annexing
Canada](https://www.barrons.com/news/trump-jokes-canada-becoming-51st-us-state-a-great-idea-ed5c760f)---has
generated significant attention and speculation about the incoming
administration's plans. Among these imperialist ambitions, the
[**reclamation** of the Panama Canal stands out as a **focal** point of
immediate and relevant strategic significance]{.underline}. On Tuesday,
Trump deliberately refused to rule out the use of [military
force](https://www.nytimes.com/2025/01/07/us/politics/trump-panama-canal-greenland.html),
preserving maximum leverage in what has so far been a one-sided
negotiation.

[Why is [Trump **focused** on]{.mark} these [issues
now]{.mark}]{.underline}? The incoming president's remarks likely
represent a calculated [two-level
game](https://sciencepolicy.colorado.edu/students/envs_5720/putnam_1988.pdf) strategy:
Internationally, [Trump is looking [to tackle rising canal]{.mark}
transit [**fees** while **countering** China's]{.mark} expanding
[influence]{.mark}]{.underline} in the Western Hemisphere. Domestically,
this rhetoric appeals to his political base. While the abrupt and public
expressions of displeasure regarding Panama and the canal risk
undermining hemispheric stability, [legitimate [concerns about
**growing** Chinese influence]{.mark} over the canal **[demand]{.mark}**
Washington's [attention]{.mark}]{.underline} and warrant a measured,
diplomatic approach.

Intervention to sovereignty

The Panama Canal's origins and US intervention are inextricably
intertwined. After failed negotiations with Colombia, the United States
pivoted to support Panamanian independence in 1903, with President
Theodore Roosevelt employing "[gun boat
diplomacy](https://hamptonroadsnavalmuseum.blogspot.com/2015/09/uss-nashville-pg-7-and-building-of.html)"
to deter Colombian resistance to the movement. The reward for military
support and US recognition of the Republic of Panama was
the [Hay-Bunau-Varilla
Treaty](https://avalon.law.yale.edu/20th_century/pan001.asp), which
granted the United States perpetual control over a ten-mile-wide canal
zone for [ten million
dollars](https://avalon.law.yale.edu/20th_century/pan001.asp#art14) down
and \$250,000 a year. After considerable investment of
US [blood](https://www.history.com/news/panama-canal-construction-dangers) and [treasure](https://pancanal.com/en/end-of-the-construction/),
the US-built canal remains a remarkable engineering achievement and a
critical artery of global trade. However, the 1903 political arrangement
sowed deep-seated resentment over perceived infringements on Panamanian
sovereignty.

In 1977, President Jimmy Carter championed the Torrijos-Carter Treaties,
aiming to foster goodwill and strengthen US-Panama relations. To secure
Senate ratification, two treaties were negotiated: one for transfer of
the canal by the turn of the millennium and the other to ensure
permanent neutrality. The Senate ratified the treaties by the [slimmest
of
margins](https://history.state.gov/milestones/1977-1980/panama-canal#:~:text=The%20Senate%20ratified%20the%20Neutrality,for%20the%20Torrijos%2DCarter%20Treaties.).
On December 31, 1999, Panama assumed control of the canal.

[[The]{.mark} Panama [Canal is **vital** for]{.mark} global [shipping,
offering **unmatched** efficiency]{.mark} for trade between Asia and the
Americas' eastern ports]{.underline}. Alternatives such as overland
transport or navigating Cape Horn add significant distance, cost, and
environmental impact, while [[Arctic routes are **seasonal** and
**geopolitically constrained**]{.underline}]{.mark}. As the most
reliable maritime link between the Atlantic and Pacific, [the canal
remains **irreplaceable**]{.underline}.

China's expanding influence

[[Trump's]{.mark} recent [comments]{.mark} on]{.underline} the Panama
Canal's [transit fees and Chinese influence]{.underline} have [[thrust
the canal to]{.mark} the [**forefront** of]{.mark} US [strategic
discourse. Chinese companies]{.mark}]{.underline} such as [Landbridge
Group](https://www.landbridge.com.hk/about.html) and the Hong
Kong-based [CK Hutchison
Holdings](https://www.ckh.com.hk/en/global/home.php) [now [[operate]{.mark}
ports](https://www.csis.org/analysis/key-decision-point-coming-panama-canal) [at
**both ends**]{.mark} of the canal]{.underline}. This [[presence raises
concerns about]{.mark} potential [**dual-use** infra]{.mark}structure
[and **strategic maneuvering**]{.mark}]{.underline}, particularly given
China's [deepening
ties](https://dialogue.earth/en/business/26121-belt-and-road-the-new-face-of-china-in-latin-america/) to
Latin America.

The United States wields significant economic leverage over Panama. As
the primary user of the canal and Panama's largest provider of foreign
direct investment---[\$3.8
billion](https://www.state.gov/u-s-relations-with-panama/#:~:text=The%20United%20States%20provides%20more%20foreign%20direct%20investment,direct%20investment%20position%20of%20%243.8%20billion%20in%202022.) annually---the
United States can influence Panamanian decision making. Conversely, the
United States and its partners present few viable alternatives to
Chinese investment in the region, a reality unlikely to change in the
short term. Will Panama prioritize alignment with US interests to
safeguard this support, or will it risk economic repercussions by
favoring China and leveraging its control over this vital trade route?
Alternatively, could US economic retaliation, Panama's reaction, and
Chinese competition escalate tensions to the point that interventions
are warranted under the treaty?

[Transit
fees](https://www.argusmedia.com/en/news-and-insights/latest-market-news/2641684-trump-panama-tiff-highlights-rising-transit-cost),
which are calculated using a universally applicable formula, have surged
in recent years. In part, these fee increases are a response to droughts
in 2023 and early 2024, which restricted the number of ships that could
transit the canal. Authorities raised fees to counteract revenue loss
from the transit restrictions. Nonetheless, if costs continue to rise,
and if China continues to expand its presence around the canal, then
there may be louder calls to resurrect the [Roosevelt
Corollary](https://www.theodorerooseveltcenter.org/Learn-About-TR/TR-Encyclopedia/Foreign-Affairs/Roosevelt-Corollary) to
the Monroe Doctrine. This corollary asserts the United States' right to
intervene in the region to ensure stability and prevent foreign
interference.

The legal framework

Trump's claim that the Panama Canal Treaties were a "[bad
part](https://www.politico.com/live-updates/2025/01/07/congress/trump-jimmy-carter-panama-canal-00196862)" of
the Carter legacy is a simplification of the nuanced legal and
geopolitical complexities surrounding the Panama Canal. The [Permanent
Neutrality
Treaty](https://pancanal.com/wp-content/uploads/2022/07/neutrality-treaty.pdf) imposed
obligations on both parties. An argument can certainly be made that
Panama has, or is close to, breaching some of its treaty obligations:

[Transit
fees](https://2001-2009.state.gov/p/wha/rlnks/11936.htm): Panama
committed to ensuring that tolls and related charges for transit would
remain "just, reasonable, equitable, and consistent with international
law." US
Senate [consent](https://2001-2009.state.gov/p/wha/rlnks/11936.htm) to
the treaty was predicated on an understanding that fee-setting would
consider US interests, including factors such as the feasibility of
alternative transportation modes and the goal of maximizing US
international commerce. While the shipping fees may be flag-agnostic,
they have a disproportionate impact on US shipping. Interpretations of
reasonableness may vary but it is clear that Trump views current fees
paid by US companies as
"[exorbitant](https://www.cnn.com/2024/12/23/politics/panama-canal-history-trump/index.html)."

[Neutrality](https://2001-2009.state.gov/p/wha/rlnks/11936.htm): Both
parties agreed to maintain the canal's permanent neutrality with
particular emphasis on ensuring access for military vessels.
[[China's]{.mark} economic [control]{.mark}]{.underline} on both sides
of the canal [[raises]{.mark} concerns about]{.underline} the
[[potential for **rapid militarization** and]{.underline}]{.mark} its
[[ability to control canal **access**. Panama's willingness to
**relinquish**]{.mark} critical economic [control]{.mark} of
strategically significant areas]{.underline} and infrastructure---a
hallmark of [China's Belt and Road
Initiative](https://www.washingtonexaminer.com/news/world/3264659/china-belt-and-road-initiative-outpaces-us-influence/) strategy---[[casts
doubt on]{.mark} Panama's]{.underline} resolve and [[capacity to]{.mark}
effectively **[safeguard]{.mark}** the canal's
[neutrality]{.mark}]{.underline} as agreed to in the treaty. The costs
of a neutrality breach are significant enough that the United States may
be justified in taking preemptive action.

The rhetoric, while appealing to nationalist sentiments, risks
undermining decades of diplomacy, established international law, and
US-Panama relations. However, [[Washington cannot afford to **overlook**
China's]{.mark} growing [influence]{.mark}]{.underline}, particularly
[[given]{.mark} the canal's strategic [importance in the US-China
competition]{.mark}]{.underline}---China ranks as
its [second-largest](https://www.statista.com/statistics/710499/share-cargo-volume-panama-canal-by-country/#:~:text=In%20the%20fiscal%20year%202021,through%20the%20Canal%20that%20year.) customer.
[[Trump]{.mark}'s statements]{.underline} likely [[aim to]{.mark}
pressure Panama on]{.underline} transit [tariffs, [**caution** Panama
on]{.mark} increased reliance and [**cooperation** with China, and
**project** US resolve]{.mark}]{.underline}. To protect its interests
without destabilizing the region, [the **U**]{.underline}nited
**[S]{.underline}**tates [must approach this situation with **strategic
foresight** and **diplomatic precision**]{.underline}.

#### Expanded Chinese influence collapses US military readiness

\--enables a deniable blockade on U.S. military logistics during
conflict\
\--collapsing global deterrence\
\--accelerates great power war

Yovani E. Chavez **Rodriguez** **and** Magnus **Mohlin 23**,
\[Rodriguez: PhD in Public International Law from the University of
Oslo, Professor at Universidad de las Americas in Ecuador; Mohlin:
Senior Researcher at the Swedish Defense Research Agency\], "The Panama
Canal -- China's Illusive Chess Piece: What China's Presence in the
Panama Canal May Mean for Regional and Global Security," *Comparative
Strategy*, 42:4, 467-488, DOI: 10.1080/01495933.2023.2219190, July 13,
2023,
<https://www.researchgate.net/publication/372338930_The_Panama_Canal_-_China%27s_illusive_chess_piece_What_China%27s_presence_in_the_Panama_Canal_may_mean_for_regional_and_global_security>

[[China's]{.mark} latent [choke point on]{.mark} the US - the [Panama
Canal]{.mark}]{.underline}

The [[risk]{.underline}]{.mark} is, of course, that Chinese control over
the entry ports to the canal can lead to something akin to a [[naval
blockade]{.mark} of the entire canal,]{.underline} but without the
apparent use of military force. Already in 1998, US Admiral Thomas
Moorer alerted the US Senate to the possibility [that [Chinese port
operations in Panama]{.mark} could [block American military supplies and
warships in]{.mark} case of a [conflict]{.mark}]{.underline}. According
to him, such an operation [could [also affect]{.mark} the possibility of
supplying [deployed forces]{.mark} in the Mediterranean Sea and the
Persian Gulf, [and]{.mark} as a consequence **[lead to serious
constraints to]{.mark}** their **[combat
readiness]{.mark}**]{.underline}.71

In 1999, US Senator Helen Chenoweth promoted US House of Representative
Joint Resolution 77 to warn the US Senate about the increasing Chinese
control of maritime ports and military presence in the Panama Canal.
Continuing her argument, she said it was necessary to invoke the
violation of Article V of the Neutrality Treaty, and to move for a
nullification of the Treaty. Article V of this Treaty states that only
the Republic of Panama maintain military forces and installations to
defend the Panama Canal.72 In consequence, she argued, Hutchison Whampoa
cannot be regarded as anything but a corporate cutout of the PLA.73

Similarly, in 1999, US Senator Trent Lott warned that [[China]{.mark}
through Hutchison Whampoa was [endanger]{.mark}ing [US national security
for]{.mark} gaining [port control]{.mark}]{.underline} through its
presence at both ends of the Panama Canal.74 Moreover, in 1999, Howard
Phillips, the then Chairman of the Conservative Caucus, an American
public policy advocacy group, defended the idea of canceling the
Hutchison Whampoa contract to administer the Pacific and Caribbean ports
at both entries to the Panama Canal and the [[growing influence of
Communist]{.mark} Chinese economic [influence in
Panama]{.mark}]{.underline}. In consequence, he argued about the need to
negotiate the restoration of the US military in the Panama Canal to
prevent, for example, an act to Gatun dam, which [[could incapacitate
the Panama Canal and **affect US military
readiness**]{.underline}]{.mark}.75

Remarkably, not even the US intelligence community seems to have
understood the different dimensions of China's threat to the United
States in the canal zone.76 Ironically, in 1999, the US Department of
Defense claimed that Hutchison Whampoa was not a threat to US national
security.77 Interestingly, and conversely, the commanding officer of
USSOUTHCOM General Laura Richardson warned that [[Chinese companies
could be **weaponized and quickly be utilized as military capabilities**
in the Panama Canal]{.mark} when necessary.78]{.underline}

That the [[security]{.mark} in and [around the canal]{.mark} has been
allowed to be [compromised in this manner has **ramifications not only
in the global context, but also in the regional**]{.mark}. As a result,
[any naval blockade by China]{.mark} could [lead to military
actions]{.mark} in Panama]{.underline}. Based on the Neutrality Treaty,
the United States of America has the right to take military actions to
reopen the Canal or restore the operations of the Canal, when
necessary.79

Consequently, it is necessary for us to go a bit further and to reflect
on the magnitude of the hold that China currently has over the canal. We
argue that this hold has four very important modalities and that China
operates her companies in a very strategic way in order to increase its
grip over the canal. The first is through its non-commitment to the
Panama Canal treaties in effect today, while the second is through
China's deep economic ties with Panama. The third and fourth aspects are
intertwined in the sense that the Chinese companies operating the ports
in and around the canal are closely connected to the PLA.

First, [[China's legal framework could **jeopardize US national
security**]{.underline}]{.mark} in the coming future because China has
not signed the 1977 Neutrality Treaty.80 Article II of this Treaty holds
that the neutrality concerns the transit of vessels from all countries,
and under all conditions, and finally that the Isthmus of Panama shall
not be targeted [[in an armed conflict]{.mark}.81 However, and this
could perhaps be the core of the problem at hand, China, as the only
permanent member of the Security Council, has not signed this Treaty.
China's reluctance to recognize the Panama Canal Neutrality Treaty is an
issue to consider for the integrity of the Panama Canal and the region.
As a result, [China is not legally bound to respect Panama Canal
Neutrality in case of]{.mark} armed conflict or
[war]{.mark}]{.underline}. In April 2022, Panamanian officials traveled
to China to formally request a negotiating table between Panama and
China to convince China to sign the Neutrality Treaty.82 Moreover,
[[China's strategic alliance with like-minded states]{.mark} could lead
countries [to]{.mark} cause **[miscalc]{.mark}**ulated [security
risks]{.mark}]{.underline} in the region. For instance, while China has
provided training for the Cuban Revolutionary Armed Forces (CRAF) to
suppress mass riots, it has also provided Venezuela with political and
economic support.83 Also, in 2021, United States warned Cuba and
Venezuela about the danger for countries in the Americas due to the
possible arrival of Iranian vessels that were allegedly carrying
weapons.84

Second, [[China's increasing economic relation with Panama could evolve
into]{.mark} even closer integration of the Panama Canal into the global
network known as [China's New Silk Road]{.mark}]{.underline}. In 2014,
and as mentioned above, a Chinese diplomatic and economic delegation
visited the PCA to express its interest in designing, constructing, and
financing a Fourth Set of Locks in the Panama Canal.85 Thus, when Panama
and China formalized diplomatic relations, the legal pathway for direct
involvement in the Panama Canal was furthered even more through
bilateral agreements. To this effect, China-Panama Bilateral Agreement
XIX states that both countries, in agreement with China's BRI and MSR,
"will conduct cooperation and exchanges of infrastructure connectivity
in areas of mutual interest, such as roads, railways, bridges, civil
aviation, ports, canals, and energy."86 According to this agreement, any
integration should be through information sharing, personnel exchange
and training, joint research, and capacity building.

As of 2022, the PCA is analyzing the construction of the Fourth Set of
Locks, which is a vital future project for the Panama Canal. However,
China's interest in the Comparative Strategy 479 Fourth Set of Locks of
the Panama Canal seems that it slowed down but considering China's
long-term planning is a matter of time to reactive conversations about
this important project. However, contrary to China's interest on this
project, the [[U]{.mark}nited [S]{.mark}tates is apparently
[lag]{.mark}ging [behind China]{.mark}]{.underline} because no American
business or diplomatic delegations have publicly announced their
interest on the Fourth Sect of Lock, which is a vital need for the
Panama Canal in the future.

Thirdly, due to [[low trust in Chinese ports in Panama]{.mark}, US
warships tend to avoid those ports, a fact that seriously
**[hampers]{.mark} the [ability to execute]{.mark}
[operations]{.mark}**. In that regard, the PATSA Port is most common
suggested port [for US warships]{.mark}. In 2019, US Admiral Gary
Roughead argued that US warship regularly do not call to Chinese ports
due to the risk that [American cybersecurity systems]{.mark} could be
[at risk]{.mark} of being exposed [to **malicious commercial port
information-technology**]{.mark} (IT) [systems]{.mark}]{.underline}.87
Without knowing the exact circumstances leading to US Navy guided
missile destroyer USS Zumwalt suffering a propulsion failure in 2016
while crossing the Panama Canal to the Pacific Ocean (actually requiring
it to be towed to the PATSA Port),88 security in the canal must be
regarded as an important aspect of US national security.

Fourth, [[China's grip]{.mark} through its multinational companies [in
the Panama Canal is]{.mark} still [a matter of
concern]{.mark}]{.underline}. According to former commander of US
Transportation Command (USTRANSCOM), General Stephen Lyons, COSCO
performs sealift as a sort of out-sourced logistical support to PLA
operations.89 COSCO is one of the most prominent Chinese companies in
charge of port operations and international maritime logistics with
connections that, at least according to what some sources claim, has
paramilitary capabilities to support PLA operations.90 As noted by the
2022 Report on Military and Security Developments Involving the People's
Republic of China, **[[China actively pursues military-civil
fusion]{.underline}]{.mark}** through state-owned companies like
COSCO.91 It should be noted that COSCO, with its strong ties to China's
military industries, provides logistics and supply support for PLA's
operations worldwide.92

The relationship between PLA and Chinese companies is an interesting and
important topic in itself. According to some sources, the
[[PLA]{.underline}]{.mark} has [[embarked]{.underline}]{.mark} on \"the
**[[biggest]{.underline}]{.mark}** peacetime **[[naval build-up the
world has ever seen]{.underline}]{.mark}**," and while doing so has
involved the private sector in a way that in China is referred to as a
"military-civil fusion." In that sense, many Chinese commercial
entities, extending to and including shipping companies, are said to
form "a key part of the integrated military-civilian logistics system."
The PLA Navy (PLAN) may at the moment have only have one overseas
military base, in Djibouti, but "replenishment and other logistical
support is in theory available wherever Cosco operates," and its "global
network \[...\] allows the \[PLAN\] to resolve logistics and supply
issues outside China's immediate maritime periphery."93

It should also, in this context, be noted that the PCA has two important
boards. The most important is the Directive Board with its 11 Panamanian
members oversees establishing policies for operations and supervising
Panama Canal management. While they serve nine years each of them, ten
are appointed by the executive branch and one by the legislative
branch.94 The other board is the Advisory Board, which serves as a
consulting body to make recommendations for the Directive Board and the
Administration of the Panama Canal. The Advisory Board has 16 foreign
members from different sectors who are appointed by the Directive Board
to serve five years. Not surprisingly, China has two members in the
Advisory Board. Chee Chen Tung 480 Y. E. CHAVEZ RODRIGUEZ AND M. MOHLIN
who has served for 23 years since 1999 and Wang Haimin from COSCO who
has served three years since 2019.95

Likewise, China's port control in Panama until 2047 is yet another
issue. In that regard, Hutchison Whampoa, which controls Hutchison
Panama Ports, has Chinese businessman Li Ka-shing as its senior
advisor.96 Since 1999, Li Ka-shing has known for the US Congress for his
alleged links to the PLA and the Chinese Communist Party.97 Although
those claims have not been corroborated, Xi Jinping's new strategy
requires all Chinese, be it companies or individuals, to be loyal to,
and support Chinese Communist Party policies. Therefore, it was not
surprising that Li Ka-shing showed public support for China's new legal
framework for Hong Kong in 2020 despite concern of western countries for
[[China's **repressing policies**]{.underline}]{.mark} to ban sedition,
secession [[or]{.underline}]{.mark} subversion.98 Still, there is not
clarity how Chinese **[[Communist]{.underline}]{.mark}** Party's
**[[pressures]{.underline}]{.mark}** will [[affect]{.mark}]{.underline}
Hutchison Whampoa's decision-making process in relation to the [[Panama
Canal]{.mark}; however, attention should be devoted]{.underline}.

### 1nc -- Democratic Backsliding

#### Trump is [cracking down]{.underline} on Chinese influence now\-\--that prevents [dual-use]{.underline} and rapid militarization of the Canal.

**Curley '25** \[Gregg; 1/9/25; Senior US Marine Corps Fellow, JD from
Roger Williams University, MA in Military Operational Art and Science
from the Air Command and Staff College, MA in Military Studies from the
Marine Corps Command and Staff College; Atlantic Council, "The US is
right to be concerned about China's influence over the Panama Canal,"
https://www.atlanticcouncil.org/blogs/new-atlanticist/right-to-be-concerned-about-chinas-influence-over-the-panama-canal/\]

President-elect Donald [Trump's]{.underline} recent [rhetoric
on]{.underline} territorial ambitions in the Western
Hemisphere---ranging from [retaking the Panama
Canal](https://www.msn.com/en-au/news/other/trump-threatens-to-retake-panama-canal-in-truth-social-post/ar-AA1wir1S?ocid=BingNewsSerp) and [buying
Greenland](https://apnews.com/article/trump-greenland-norway-panama-canal-canada-a52858e3075f9b5ad95e78753293fc1f) to [annexing
Canada](https://www.barrons.com/news/trump-jokes-canada-becoming-51st-us-state-a-great-idea-ed5c760f)---has
generated significant attention and speculation about the incoming
administration's plans. Among these imperialist ambitions, the
[**reclamation** of the Panama Canal stands out as a **focal** point of
immediate and relevant strategic significance]{.underline}. On Tuesday,
Trump deliberately refused to rule out the use of [military
force](https://www.nytimes.com/2025/01/07/us/politics/trump-panama-canal-greenland.html),
preserving maximum leverage in what has so far been a one-sided
negotiation.

[Why is [Trump **focused** on]{.mark} these [issues
now]{.mark}]{.underline}? The incoming president's remarks likely
represent a calculated [two-level
game](https://sciencepolicy.colorado.edu/students/envs_5720/putnam_1988.pdf) strategy:
Internationally, [Trump is looking [to tackle rising canal]{.mark}
transit [**fees** while **countering** China's]{.mark} expanding
[influence]{.mark}]{.underline} in the Western Hemisphere. Domestically,
this rhetoric appeals to his political base. While the abrupt and public
expressions of displeasure regarding Panama and the canal risk
undermining hemispheric stability, [legitimate [concerns about

The Panama Canal's origins and US intervention are inextricably
intertwined. After failed negotiations with Colombia, the United States
pivoted to support Panamanian independence in 1903, with President
Theodore Roosevelt employing "[gun boat
diplomacy](https://hamptonroadsnavalmuseum.blogspot.com/2015/09/uss-nashville-pg-7-and-building-of.html)"
to deter Colombian resistance to the movement. The reward for military
support and US recognition of the Republic of Panama was
the [Hay-Bunau-Varilla
Treaty](https://avalon.law.yale.edu/20th_century/pan001.asp), which
granted the United States perpetual control over a ten-mile-wide canal
zone for [ten million
dollars](https://avalon.law.yale.edu/20th_century/pan001.asp#art14) down
and \$250,000 a year. After considerable investment of
US [blood](https://www.history.com/news/panama-canal-construction-dangers) and [treasure](https://pancanal.com/en/end-of-the-construction/),
the US-built canal remains a remarkable engineering achievement and a
critical artery of global trade. However, the 1903 political arrangement
sowed deep-seated resentment over perceived infringements on Panamanian
sovereignty.

In 1977, President Jimmy Carter championed the Torrijos-Carter Treaties,
aiming to foster goodwill and strengthen US-Panama relations. To secure
Senate ratification, two treaties were negotiated: one for transfer of
the canal by the turn of the millennium and the other to ensure
permanent neutrality. The Senate ratified the treaties by the [slimmest
of
margins](https://history.state.gov/milestones/1977-1980/panama-canal#:~:text=The%20Senate%20ratified%20the%20Neutrality,for%20the%20Torrijos%2DCarter%20Treaties.).
On December 31, 1999, Panama assumed control of the canal.

[[The]{.mark} Panama [Canal is **vital** for]{.mark} global [shipping,
offering **unmatched** efficiency]{.mark} for trade between Asia and the

transport or navigating Cape Horn add significant distance, cost, and

**geopolitically constrained**]{.underline}]{.mark}. As the most
reliable maritime link between the Atlantic and Pacific, [the canal
remains **irreplaceable**]{.underline}.

China's expanding influence

[[Trump's]{.mark} recent [comments]{.mark} on]{.underline} the Panama
Canal's [transit fees and Chinese influence]{.underline} have [[thrust
the canal to]{.mark} the [**forefront** of]{.mark} US [strategic
discourse. Chinese companies]{.mark}]{.underline} such as [Landbridge
Group](https://www.landbridge.com.hk/about.html) and the Hong
Kong-based [CK Hutchison
Holdings](https://www.ckh.com.hk/en/global/home.php) [now [[operate]{.mark}
ports](https://www.csis.org/analysis/key-decision-point-coming-panama-canal) [at
**both ends**]{.mark} of the canal]{.underline}. This [[presence raises
concerns about]{.mark} potential [**dual-use** infra]{.mark}structure
[and **strategic maneuvering**]{.mark}]{.underline}, particularly given
China's [deepening
ties](https://dialogue.earth/en/business/26121-belt-and-road-the-new-face-of-china-in-latin-america/) to
Latin America.

The United States wields significant economic leverage over Panama. As
the primary user of the canal and Panama's largest provider of foreign
direct investment---[\$3.8
billion](https://www.state.gov/u-s-relations-with-panama/#:~:text=The%20United%20States%20provides%20more%20foreign%20direct%20investment,direct%20investment%20position%20of%20%243.8%20billion%20in%202022.) annually---the
United States can influence Panamanian decision making. Conversely, the
United States and its partners present few viable alternatives to
Chinese investment in the region, a reality unlikely to change in the
short term. Will Panama prioritize alignment with US interests to
safeguard this support, or will it risk economic repercussions by
favoring China and leveraging its control over this vital trade route?
Alternatively, could US economic retaliation, Panama's reaction, and
Chinese competition escalate tensions to the point that interventions
are warranted under the treaty?

[Transit
fees](https://www.argusmedia.com/en/news-and-insights/latest-market-news/2641684-trump-panama-tiff-highlights-rising-transit-cost),
which are calculated using a universally applicable formula, have surged
in recent years. In part, these fee increases are a response to droughts
in 2023 and early 2024, which restricted the number of ships that could
transit the canal. Authorities raised fees to counteract revenue loss
from the transit restrictions. Nonetheless, if costs continue to rise,
and if China continues to expand its presence around the canal, then
there may be louder calls to resurrect the [Roosevelt
Corollary](https://www.theodorerooseveltcenter.org/Learn-About-TR/TR-Encyclopedia/Foreign-Affairs/Roosevelt-Corollary) to
the Monroe Doctrine. This corollary asserts the United States' right to
intervene in the region to ensure stability and prevent foreign
interference.

The legal framework

Trump's claim that the Panama Canal Treaties were a "[bad
part](https://www.politico.com/live-updates/2025/01/07/congress/trump-jimmy-carter-panama-canal-00196862)" of
the Carter legacy is a simplification of the nuanced legal and
geopolitical complexities surrounding the Panama Canal. The [Permanent
Neutrality
Treaty](https://pancanal.com/wp-content/uploads/2022/07/neutrality-treaty.pdf) imposed
obligations on both parties. An argument can certainly be made that
Panama has, or is close to, breaching some of its treaty obligations:

[Transit
fees](https://2001-2009.state.gov/p/wha/rlnks/11936.htm): Panama
committed to ensuring that tolls and related charges for transit would
remain "just, reasonable, equitable, and consistent with international
law." US
Senate [consent](https://2001-2009.state.gov/p/wha/rlnks/11936.htm) to
the treaty was predicated on an understanding that fee-setting would
consider US interests, including factors such as the feasibility of
alternative transportation modes and the goal of maximizing US
international commerce. While the shipping fees may be flag-agnostic,
they have a disproportionate impact on US shipping. Interpretations of
reasonableness may vary but it is clear that Trump views current fees
paid by US companies as
"[exorbitant](https://www.cnn.com/2024/12/23/politics/panama-canal-history-trump/index.html)."

[Neutrality](https://2001-2009.state.gov/p/wha/rlnks/11936.htm): Both
parties agreed to maintain the canal's permanent neutrality with
particular emphasis on ensuring access for military vessels.
[[China's]{.mark} economic [control]{.mark}]{.underline} on both sides
of the canal [[raises]{.mark} concerns about]{.underline} the
[[potential for **rapid militarization** and]{.underline}]{.mark} its
[[ability to control canal **access**. Panama's willingness to
**relinquish**]{.mark} critical economic [control]{.mark} of
strategically significant areas]{.underline} and infrastructure---a
hallmark of [China's Belt and Road
Initiative](https://www.washingtonexaminer.com/news/world/3264659/china-belt-and-road-initiative-outpaces-us-influence/) strategy---[[casts
doubt on]{.mark} Panama's]{.underline} resolve and [[capacity to]{.mark}
effectively **[safeguard]{.mark}** the canal's
[neutrality]{.mark}]{.underline} as agreed to in the treaty. The costs
of a neutrality breach are significant enough that the United States may
be justified in taking preemptive action.

The rhetoric, while appealing to nationalist sentiments, risks
undermining decades of diplomacy, established international law, and
US-Panama relations. However, [[Washington cannot afford to **overlook**
China's]{.mark} growing [influence]{.mark}]{.underline}, particularly
[[given]{.mark} the canal's strategic [importance in the US-China
competition]{.mark}]{.underline}---China ranks as
its [second-largest](https://www.statista.com/statistics/710499/share-cargo-volume-panama-canal-by-country/#:~:text=In%20the%20fiscal%20year%202021,through%20the%20Canal%20that%20year.) customer.
[[Trump]{.mark}'s statements]{.underline} likely [[aim to]{.mark}
pressure Panama on]{.underline} transit [tariffs, [**caution** Panama
on]{.mark} increased reliance and [**cooperation** with China, and
**project** US resolve]{.mark}]{.underline}. To protect its interests
without destabilizing the region, [the **U**]{.underline}nited
**[S]{.underline}**tates [must approach this situation with **strategic
foresight** and **diplomatic precision**]{.underline}.

#### Expanded Chinese presence over the Canal causes democratic backsliding

**Berg and Ziemer** 20**24** \[Ryan; director of the Americas Program
and head of the Future of Venezuela Initiative at the Center for
Strategic and International Studies; Henry; associate fellow with the
Americas Program at the Center for Strategic and International Studies;
"Exporting Autocracy China's Role in Democratic Backsliding in Latin
America and the Caribbean" CSIS; 2/15;
https://www.csis.org/analysis/exporting-autocracy\]

**LAC = Latin America and the Caribbean**

China has proven adept at using this perception---and the United States'
comparative lack of a substantial presence in citizen security
efforts---to bolster its ties with national police forces.34
[[China]{.mark} has [conduct]{.mark}ed at least a dozen bilateral
[exchanges]{.mark} between senior police leadership [in LAC]{.mark} and
its own internal security services, and it has led at least 10 trainings
for police forces throughout the region.]{.underline}35 Especially
concerning is the fact that [China has engaged in several **capacity-
building activities** with police forces accused of **gross human rights
abuses**]{.underline}. This includes the Cuban police, responsible for
the suppression of mass protests in the summer of 2021, as well as the
Nicaraguan National Police, a force which China has delivered arms and
equipment to and which is under international sanction for its role in
brutally crushing popular demonstrations starting in 2018.36 Just as
China often serves as a lender of last resort to countries isolated from
global financial or business markets, [so too is it rapidly emerging as
a **security partner for authoritarian regimes,** willing to help
augment the capabilities of these governments to maintain control over
their populations through surveillance and brute force
alike]{.underline}.37

[China's trade and investment strategy has also paid dividends in the
security and defense space, most notably in the form of China's control
of port infrastructure throughout the hemisphere.]{.underline}
[[Maritime ports]{.mark} owned or [operated by China]{.mark} often
[serve]{.mark} dual civilian and **[military
uses]{.mark}**]{.underline}.38 They are [[instrumental nodes of
trade]{.mark} and commerce in peacetime, but [in]{.mark} moments of
crisis or [conflict, they could **provide an array of military
advantages.**]{.mark}]{.underline} Most directly, PRC-built ports around
the world have been built to specifications, particularly the berth of
docking spaces, allowing warships to dock and conduct resupply. [Such
[capabilities are **essential**]{.mark}, as the PLA looks to transform
itself from a force with limited regional power-projection capabilities
[to a military capable of global expeditionary operations with a **true
"blue water" navy.**]{.mark}]{.underline}

Outside of directly supplying ships, however, [[China's grip over key
LAC ports is a **powerful intelligence-gathering
tool.**]{.underline}]{.mark}39 [[Port operators have access to
**critical data** on]{.mark} the origins, destinations, and types of
[cargo moving through their docks: **vital information for the PRC to
understand** supply chain shifts, demand for certain types of resources,
and, in the case of ports **along maritime chokepoints like the Panama
Canal**]{.mark} or Strait of Magellan, [the location of military vessels
en route from Atlantic to Pacific.]{.mark} Crucially[, this form of
influence **does not require China to have an active military presence
in the Western Hemisphere,**]{.mark}]{.underline} a development which
would surely inflame tensions with the United States and even backfire
in terms of China's relationship with the region. Instead, leveraging
this data would simply require invocation of the National Intelligence
Law to compel port operators to turn over defense-relevant data to
Beijing, thereby granting China a passive capability close to the
epicenter of U.S. military activity.40

China has made additional forays into forms of engagement traditionally
thought of as defense cooperation. Amid Russian defense industrial
shortfalls, China has surged to become the world's fifth-largest arms
supplier, with countries in LAC such as Bolivia and Venezuela purchasing
substantial quantities of Chinese arms already.41 The recent row over
Argentina's consideration of the JF-17, a joint Pakistani-Chinese
fighter jet, for its air force illustrates the growing efforts by China
to internationalize its arms industry. This also includes professional
military education, where China has been making strides to displace the
United States from its long-held role as primary source of foreign
training and assistance to LAC militaries.42 China has pursued a
concerted strategy in this regard to overmatch the United States in
quantity (though certainly not quality, for the time being) of training
provided. Whereas U.S. service academies may have billets for just a
handful of officers from a given LAC country, institutions like the
China National Defense University will accept delegations of a dozen
officers or more for instruction, often centered around sectors of
particular import to the PLA such as cybersecurity.43 Furthermore, this
professional military education is essential for identifying and
cultivating talent among junior officers, many of whom will eventually
Ryan C. Berg and Henry Ziemer \| 8 rise to senior positions in their
home militaries where they may be in positions to shape opinion and
policy toward the PRC.

Accordingly, while China's security and defense engagement has a long
way to go before it achieves the same level of maturity as its economic
efforts, expansion in this sector is potentially even more concerning
from a U.S. perspective.

Propagation and Protection

This report hypothesizes that [[China's engagement in LAC **encourages
democratic backsliding**]{.mark} along two key directions: [propagation
of authoritarian principles and protection of backsliding governments
from the consequences of their actions.]{.mark} This [enables
**authoritarian**]{.mark} **or semi- authoritarian [regimes]{.mark}** to
persist in the face of external---usually regional---pressure to reform.
Propagation refers to the means by which China seeks to encourage the
adoption of authoritarian political systems through its international
relations. In this regard, it is perhaps the most misunderstood vector
through which China contributes to democratic backsliding.]{.underline}
Indeed, China's engagement across LAC suggests that it does not
condition its engagement on whether a country is democratic or
autocratic. Yet even if the PRC is agnostic as to the types of regimes
it engages with, this does not mean that Beijing does not prefer certain
types of governments and would like to these see those types of regimes
proliferate throughout the region. Indeed, as a number of scholars of
great power politics and democracy alike have argued, states
consistently seek to sow their own governance models well beyond their
borders. Kevin Narizny, for instance, contends that the spread of
democracy throughout the world is conditioned upon Anglo-American
primacy within the international system, as both the United Kingdom and
later United States sought to promote their political models abroad
among their colonies, clients, and allies.81

While under Deng Xiaoping, China pursued a strategy known famously as
"biding time" and "hiding strength," this policy has changed drastically
under Xi Jinping. Whereas China was once reticent to admit there was a
"China model," it has recently become more vocal about its domestic
model as an alternative to Western liberal democracy. This forms the
basis for the PRC's "discursive competition," which takes explicit aim
at concepts such as international civil and political rights while
promoting the primacy of collective rights in line with the CCP's
centralized and state-driven model for economic and social
development.82 China has promoted its discursive competition in LAC,
most visibly in reports from individuals who have attended trainings
hosted by the PRC. Journalists, for instance, have noted that the
Chinese trainings encourage favorable coverage of the state, while in
the military dimension, [[SOUTHCOM has reported that]{.mark} at least
one of [China's trainings]{.mark} has [sought to discourage participants
from seeing the U]{.mark}nited [S]{.mark}tates [as an effective security
partner.]{.mark}]{.underline}83

The GSI perhaps best embodies China's evolving perception of its role in
world affairs.84 The initiative professes to merge questions of domestic
and international security, "coordinating between China's domestic
security and the common security of the world."85 As one analyst notes,
the GSI seeks "security at home, influence abroad."86 Yet while the GSI
espouses a universal vision, the flavor of implementation is decidedly
in line with the CCP model of social control and, according to the
Chinese Academy of Social Sciences, "provides a classical Chinese
solution" to the problems of global instability.87 Indeed, China's
contributions to civil security in the hemisphere have stressed mass
surveillance and the proliferation of Chinese overseas police stations
and PSCs. China's international policing activities have similarly been
used not only to crack down on criminal organizations but to engage in
transnational repression by capturing and repatriating dissidents and
critics of the CCP abroad.88 [Expanding this public security approach to
the world risks exporting a similar model of restricted civil and
political rights, as well as the accretion of power in the hands of
governments.]{.underline} However, propagation has its limits. It does
not imply that China is actively and constantly seeking to convert the
countries it interacts with to autocracy. Indeed, the bulk of China's
engagement with LAC is filtered through the lens of economic interest.
But propagation does not require active intent to transpire, and many
countries have seen their democratic health and political stability
thrown into tumult as an unintended consequence of engaging with China.
Political economist Albert Hirschman has argued that trade ties between
countries over time can foster alignment among the foreign policy
systems of the trading countries.89 Given China's impressive role in
trade with the region, it is reasonable to see how LAC countries might
gradually mold themselves to become amenable to Beijing's vision of
world order, even without the CCP expressing a clear preference for a
certain regime type. At the sub-national level, for instance, the
presence of PRC companies can fuel corruption among local mayors and
governors, who use revenues and funds extracted from foreign firms to
remain in power.90 Chinese firms do not necessarily intend to undermine
local democracy; rather, they may merely wish to have productive
relations with the political power brokers responsible for regulatory
compliance and security near their operations.

The unintended consequence of this, however, [can be the [exacerbation
of]{.mark} an acute **[crisis of democracy]{.mark}** at the local level
in many countries.]{.underline}91 Certainly, even if China has no
interest in spreading its political model, [certain individuals within
the region do have an interest in mimicking the PRC.]{.underline} From
business leaders who marvel at the rapid growth of China's economy, to
public safety officials searching for answers to crime and violence, to
elected officials eager to cement their legacies with China-financed
development projects, the notion that China presents a model of
governance worthy of replicating has spread in the past two decades.92
Perhaps the best summation of this sentiment comes from Venezuelan
governor and Maduro ally Rafael Lacava, who on a November 2023 visit to
Shanghai stated, "We are in a transition and that transition looks
toward the Chinese model. That's why I'm here, that's why the president
sent me here and that's why the president has come so many times."93

Protection refers to the manner by which [[China helps to **"lock in"
authoritarian regimes** that emerge]{.mark} primarily as a result of
endogenous factors.]{.underline} [In these cases, rather than China
propagating authoritarian ideas abroad, a [country]{.mark} has [already
**ventured down the path of democratic backsliding** with little to no
influence from Beijing]{.mark}.]{.underline} Yet as the country's
leadership increasingly faces mounting domestic and international
pressure (from human rights bodies, restricted channels of global
commerce, sanctions regimes, etc.), it often turns to China as a
powerful extra-hemispheric patron to help navigate the pitfalls of
running a dictatorship. One particularly salient example is that of
Nicaragua, which until 2021 maintained no official relations with the
PRC on account of its diplomatic recognition of Taiwan. Thus, the brutal
crackdown in 2018 when the Ortega-Murillo regime murdered hundreds of
protesters, and subsequent waves of political repression, cannot be
directly attributed to PRC propagation.94 The regime's decision to drop
its diplomatic recognition of Taiwan and negotiate a free trade
agreement with China in record time has been viewed by most analysts as
a calculated step to insulate the regime from economic pressure by
trading an ally with robust liberal democratic institutions for an
authoritarian state.95 Even though China evinces no preference when it
comes to regime type, authoritarian-minded leaders throughout LAC have
come to bet on the PRC as their partner of choice.

Protection may also account for another phenomenon: authoritarian
resilience and sharing. Many of these theories look to China itself as
an autocratic regime that has successfully weathered the storms of
economic liberalization, globalization, and more recently a wave of
pandemic-induced social upheaval. This literature also notes that
attention should be paid to "deepening autocratization in already
authoritarian countries."96 Here the role of external allies is
especially important to help empower and extend the lifespan of
autocratic regimes beyond their natural lifecycle. [[China has extended
its patronage to]{.mark} all three of the [Western Hemisphere's]{.mark}
consolidated [dictatorships,]{.mark}]{.underline} in forms ranging from
economic support and assistance with sanctions evasion to technology
transfers, allowing regimes to wield the tools of digital
authoritarianism with greater finesse and at scale. In Cuba, China works
with Havana to block internet access for dissenters, and, according to
recent reports, it offered a multibillion-dollar bailout to help the
Cuban regime recover from its economic woes in exchange for greater
access to military basing infrastructure on the island.97 In Nicaragua,
China's engagement appears in its earlier stages for now, but the PRC
has already donated equipment to the country's police forces.98 In
Venezuela, Chinese arms and vehicles have been used to brutally suppress
protests, while the PRC's assistance in developing the Maduro regime's
Carnet de la Patria ("Homeland Card") ID system stands out as one of the
most sophisticated tools of digital authoritarianism in the
hemisphere.99

[Furthermore, in each of these countries, the [quality of
democracy]{.mark} as measured by the Economist Intelligence Unit has
**[declined since their inclusion into China's
BRI]{.mark}**]{.underline} (Cuba from 3.31 to 2.84, Nicaragua from 3.60
to 2.50, and Venezuela from 3.87 to 2.88).100 [These ties also seem to
cut both ways, with the stated objective of one ILD exchange between the
CCP and the Communist Party of Cuba being to "learn from Cuba's
experiences in socialist revolution and construction."]{.underline}101
This is not to suggest that China bears sole responsibility for
authoritarian retrenchment in any of these countries, but rather that
[[growing PRC engagement in]{.mark} already [authoritarian contexts
helps these regimes **continue their hold on power,**]{.mark}
potentially beyond the point at which they would be able to survive
through their own resources alone.]{.underline}

## Uniqueness

### UQ -- Military Presence

The US built the Panama Canal between 1904 and 1914. Negotiations to

#### US presence and commitment to Panama key to counterbalancing China

**Newsroom Panama 4/24**, 4-24-2025, \"Panama and the Canal vs
China/U.S. -- National Security or Economic Dominance?\",
https://newsroompanama.com/2025/04/24/panama-and-the-canal-vs-china-u-s-national-security-or-economic-dominance/

Pete Hegseth, the U.S. defense secretary, boldly said, "[The

the [two [superpowers]{.mark} had already [signed]{.mark} an

canal]{.mark}.]{.underline} "Hold up on that agreement," said Panamanian
President José Raúl Molino, denying such an agreement. Mulino further
rejected Hegseth's announcement with, "I completely reject that

between]{.mark} the [U.S. and China]{.mark} and, as well as the

is [investing]{.mark} huge amounts of money and resources [in]{.mark}

[China has an [oversized]{.mark} footprint in [military
capability]{.mark}, as well as [ground research stations]{.mark} that
enhance its space programs.]{.underline} China exploits with impunity
U.S. and global natural resources with "factory fishing fleets" that
steal food from the U.S. and other global reasons for war. Hegseth

He added that "[[war with]{.mark} [China]{.mark} is [not

Recent aerial photographs show cargo vessels docked at Balboa Port,

[engaged China in]{.mark} a [trade war with]{.mark} a [tariff]{.mark} of

gives "economic control" of the Canal. The geography doesn't really make
sense. Here's the Atlantic side of the Canal, with the Chinese-run ports
in red (obviously) and the other two in navy blue, with the little ship
icon:

And here is the Pacific side (same scale, same color scheme):

Ships going through the Canal bypass the port
operations.[4](https://www.noelmaurer.com/p/seriously-china-is-failing-to-take#footnote-4-154990971) Short
of firing non-existent missiles at passing vessels, the port operators
cannot stop them from using the passageway. The only thing the Chinese
company "controls" is Pacific-side access to the Panama Railroad. Why
that would be useful in a war is beyond me, especially when the United
States would find it trivial to get forces to Panama whereas China is
very very very far
away.[5](https://www.noelmaurer.com/p/seriously-china-is-failing-to-take#footnote-5-154990971)

#### The plan causes China fill in -- they'll expand engagement to offset US markets

JOW

[In 2000, the [Chinese market]{.mark} accounted for less than 2 percent
of Latin America's exports, but China's rapid [growth]{.mark} and
resulting demand [drove]{.mark} the region's subsequent [commodities
boom]{.mark}. Over the next eight years, [trade grew]{.mark} at an
annual rate of [31 percent]{.mark}]{.underline} \[PDF\]. By 2021, trade
exceeded \$450 billion---a [figure which grew to a record \$518 billion
in 2024]{.underline}, according to Chinese state media---and some
[[economists predict]{.mark} that it could [exceed \$700 billion]{.mark}
by 2035. [China]{.mark} currently [ranks]{.mark} as South America's [top
trading partner]{.mark} and the [second largest for Latin
America]{.mark} as a whole,]{.underline} after the United States.

Latin America's primary exports to China include soybeans and other
vegetables, animal products, copper, petroleum, oil, and other raw
materials that the country needs to drive its industrial development. In
return, the region mostly imports higher-value-added manufactured
products, a trade some experts say has undercut local industries with
cheaper Chinese goods. [As of 2024, [Beijing]{.mark} has [signed free
trade agreements]{.mark} with Chile, Costa Rica, Ecuador, Nicaragua, and
Peru]{.underline}. Trade talks with Uruguay have fallen through due to
opposition from the Mercosur trade bloc, but [[more]{.mark} than [twenty
countries]{.mark} in Latin America and the Caribbean have [signed
on]{.mark} to [China's Belt and Road Initiative]{.mark}]{.underline}
(BRI), the most recent being Colombia.

[[Chinese]{.mark} outward foreign direct
[investment]{.mark}]{.underline} (OFDI) [and loans also [play]{.mark} a
major [role in strengthening ties]{.mark} with the region]{.underline}.
In 2024, China's OFDI in Latin America and the Caribbean amounted to
roughly \$8.5 billion, or approximately 6 percent of China's total OFDI.
Meanwhile, [the state-owned [China]{.mark} Development [Bank]{.mark} and
the Export-Import Bank of China [are]{.mark} among the region's [leading
lenders]{.mark}]{.underline}; since 2005, they have loaned more than
\$120 billion to Latin American and Caribbean countries and state-owned
enterprises, often in exchange for oil and used to fund energy and
infrastructure projects. Venezuela is by far the biggest borrower; it
has received nearly \$60 billion worth of Chinese state loans, mostly
relating to energy and infrastructure. That is nearly double the amount
for the second-largest borrower, Brazil. Additionally, China is a
nonborrowing, voting member of the Inter-American Development Bank and
the Caribbean Development Bank.

At a May 2025 ministerial meeting of the China-Community of Latin
American and Caribbean States (CELAC) forum in Beijing, [[China]{.mark}
pledged to [ramp up]{.mark} its [engagement]{.mark} in the
region]{.underline}. President Xi announced a more than \$9 billion
yuan-dominated credit line to CELAC members for cooperation programs
focused on topics including development and peace and security, as well

### L -- Generic Tradeoffs

#### Melting ICE poses a great threat for the Panama Canal's dominance

**Piecyk 21** (Adrian Piecyk, Field Team Captain at Etobicoke Centre,
Managing editor of the foreign observer, Doctor of Law from University
of Toronto, bachelor's in international relations, "A Bridge Across Two
Oceans: The Arctic Challenges To Panama Shipping Canal",
https://www.thearcticinstitute.org/bridge-across-two-oceans-arctic-challenge-panama-canal-shipping/,
// AS)

If given a pair of darts and a map, you would be hard-pressed to hit
[two spots more visibly different than Panama and the Arctic
Ocean]{.underline}. These two places, disparate though they may be, are
being [[brought]{.mark} [together by]{.mark} the global threat of
[climate change]{.mark}]{.underline}. [[The disappearance of the]{.mark}
Arctic [Ocean's]{.mark} summer [ice]{.mark} over the course of the next
century [could]{.mark} also [herald the disappearance of
Panama's]{.mark} greatest [economic]{.mark} [engine]{.mark} -- [the
Panama Canal]{.mark}]{.underline} -- [[as shipping is]{.mark} instead
[redirected]{.mark} [through]{.mark} shorter [Arctic]{.mark}
[routes]{.mark}]{.underline}. The scale of this threat prompts three
questions: what advantages does Arctic shipping provide over the Panama
Canal, how is Panama confronting the issue, and how realistic is the
prospect of greater volumes of Arctic shipping? To answer these
questions, this article will first contrast the merits and importance of
both Arctic and Panama Canal shipping, followed by an examination of
Panamanian news articles and government statements, and then conclude
with a feasibility analysis and future projections. The picture which
emerges is a mixed one: Panama's media tends to focus on more immediate
threats than melting Arctic ice, and while the expansion of Arctic
shipping itself seems inevitable, it remains to be seen how quickly and
how practically it could overshadow the Canal.
