Intervention to sovereignty

The Panama [[Canal's]{.underline}]{.mark} [[origins]{.underline}]{.mark}
and US intervention are inextricably [[intertwined]{.underline}]{.mark}.
After failed negotiations with Colombia, the
[[U]{.mark}nited]{.underline} [[S]{.mark}tates]{.underline}
[[pivoted]{.underline}]{.mark} to [[support]{.underline}]{.mark}
[Panamanian]{.underline} [[independence]{.underline}]{.mark} in 1903,
with President Theodore Roosevelt employing "gun boat diplomacy" to
deter Colombian resistance to the movement. The [reward]{.underline}
[for]{.underline} military [support]{.underline} [and]{.underline} US
[recognition]{.underline} of the Republic of [Panama]{.underline} was
the Hay-Bunau-Varilla Treaty, which [granted]{.underline} the
[[U]{.mark}nited]{.underline} [[S]{.mark}tates]{.underline} perpetual
[[control]{.underline}]{.mark} [[over]{.underline}]{.mark} [a
[ten]{.underline}-[mile]{.underline}-[wide]{.underline}]{.mark} canal
[[zone]{.underline}]{.mark} [for]{.underline} [ten]{.underline}
[million]{.underline} [dollars]{.underline} down and \$250,000 a year.
After considerable investment of US blood and treasure, the US-built
canal remains a remarkable engineering achievement and a critical artery
of global trade. However, the 1903 political arrangement sowed
deep-seated resentment over perceived infringements on Panamanian
sovereignty.

In 1977, President Jimmy [[Carter]{.underline}]{.mark}
[[championed]{.underline}]{.mark} the Torrijos-Carter
[[Treaties]{.underline}]{.mark}, [aiming]{.underline} [to]{.underline}
[[foster]{.underline}]{.mark} [[goodwill]{.underline}]{.mark} and
strengthen US-Panama relations. To secure Senate ratification, two
treaties were negotiated: one for transfer of the canal by the turn of
the millennium and the other to ensure permanent neutrality. The Senate
ratified the treaties by the slimmest of margins. [On]{.underline}
[December]{.underline} [31]{.underline}, [1999]{.underline},
[[Panama]{.underline}]{.mark} [[assumed]{.underline}]{.mark}
[[control]{.underline}]{.mark} [of]{.underline} the [canal]{.underline}.

The [[Panama]{.underline}]{.mark} Canal is [[vital]{.underline}]{.mark}
[[for]{.underline}]{.mark} global [[shipping, offering unmatched
efficiency for trade]{.underline}]{.mark} between Asia and the Americas'
eastern ports. [[Alt]{.mark}ernatives]{.underline} such as overland
transport or navigating Cape Horn [[add]{.underline}]{.mark} significant
[[distance]{.underline}]{.mark}, [[cost]{.underline}]{.mark}, and
[[environmental impact, while **Arctic routes are seasonal and
geopolitically constrained.**]{.underline}]{.mark} As the most reliable
maritime link between the Atlantic and Pacific, [[the canal remains
irreplaceable.]{.underline}]{.mark}

China's expanding influence

[[Trump's]{.underline}]{.mark} recent [[comments]{.underline}]{.mark}
[on]{.mark} the Panama [[Canal's]{.underline}]{.mark} transit
[[fees]{.underline}]{.mark} [[and]{.underline}]{.mark}
[[Chinese]{.underline}]{.mark} [[influence]{.underline}]{.mark}
[have]{.underline} [[thrust]{.underline}]{.mark} the
[[canal]{.underline}]{.mark} [[to]{.underline}]{.mark} [the]{.underline}
[[forefront]{.underline}]{.mark} [[of]{.underline}]{.mark}
[US]{.underline} [[strategic]{.underline}]{.mark}
[[discourse]{.underline}]{.mark}. [[Chinese]{.underline}]{.mark}
[[companies]{.underline}]{.mark} such as Landbridge Group and the Hong
Kong-based CK Hutchison
Holdings [now]{.underline} [[operate]{.underline}]{.mark}
[ports]{.mark} at both ends of the canal. This
[[presence]{.underline}]{.mark} [[raises]{.underline}]{.mark}
[[concerns]{.underline}]{.mark} [about]{.underline} potential
dual-[use]{.underline} [infrastructure]{.underline} and strategic
maneuvering, particularly given China's deepening ties to Latin America.

The [[U]{.mark}nited]{.underline} [[S]{.mark}tates]{.underline}
[[wields]{.underline}]{.mark} significant economic
[[leverage]{.underline}]{.mark} [[over]{.underline}]{.mark}
[[Panama]{.underline}]{.mark}. As the primary user of the canal and
Panama's largest provider of foreign direct investment---\$3.8
billion annually---the [[U]{.mark}nited]{.underline}
[[S]{.mark}tates]{.underline} can [[influence]{.underline}]{.mark}
Panamanian [[decision]{.underline}]{.mark}
[[making]{.underline}]{.mark}. Conversely, the United States and its
partners present few viable alternatives to Chinese investment in the
region, a reality unlikely to change in the short term. [Will]{.mark}
[[Panama]{.underline}]{.mark} prioritize alignment with US interests to
safeguard this support, or will it [[risk]{.underline}]{.mark}
[[economic]{.underline}]{.mark} [[repercussions]{.underline}]{.mark}
[[by]{.underline}]{.mark} [[favoring]{.underline}]{.mark}
[[China]{.underline}]{.mark} [and]{.underline} [leveraging]{.underline}
its [control]{.underline} [over]{.underline} [this]{.underline} vital
trade [route]{.underline}? Alternatively, could US economic retaliation,
Panama's reaction, and Chinese competition escalate tensions to the
point that interventions are warranted under the treaty?

Transit fees, which are calculated using a universally applicable
formula, have surged in recent years. In part, these fee increases are a
response to droughts in 2023 and early 2024, which restricted the number
of ships that could transit the canal. Authorities raised fees to
counteract revenue loss from the transit restrictions. Nonetheless, if
costs continue to rise, and if China continues to expand its presence
around the canal, then there may be louder calls to resurrect the
Roosevelt Corollary to the Monroe Doctrine. This corollary asserts the
United States' right to intervene in the region to ensure stability and
prevent foreign interference. The legal framework Trump's claim that the
Panama Canal Treaties were a "bad part" of the Carter legacy is a
simplification of the nuanced legal and geopolitical complexities
surrounding the Panama Canal. The Permanent Neutrality Treaty imposed
obligations on both parties. An argument can certainly be made that
Panama has, or is close to, breaching some of its treaty obligations:
Transit fees: Panama committed to ensuring that tolls and related
charges for transit would remain "just, reasonable, equitable, and
consistent with international law." US Senate consent to the treaty was
predicated on an understanding that fee-setting would consider US
interests, including factors such as the feasibility of alternative
transportation modes and the goal of maximizing US international
commerce. While the shipping fees may be flag-agnostic, they have a
disproportionate impact on US shipping. Interpretations of
reasonableness may vary but it is clear that Trump views current fees
paid by US companies as "exorbitant."

Neutrality: Both parties agreed to maintain the canal's permanent
neutrality with particular emphasis on ensuring access for military
vessels. [[China's]{.underline}]{.mark} economic
[[control]{.underline}]{.mark} [on]{.underline} [both]{.underline}
[sides]{.underline} of the canal [raises]{.mark}
[[concerns]{.underline}]{.mark} [[about]{.underline}]{.mark} the
[[potential]{.underline}]{.mark} [[for]{.underline}]{.mark} rapid
[[militarization]{.underline}]{.mark} [[and]{.underline}]{.mark} its
[[ability]{.underline}]{.mark} [[to]{.underline}]{.mark}
[[control]{.underline}]{.mark} [[canal]{.underline}]{.mark}
[access]{.underline}. Panama's willingness to relinquish critical
economic control of strategically significant areas and
infrastructure---a hallmark of China's Belt and Road
Initiative strategy---casts doubt on Panama's resolve and capacity to
effectively safeguard the canal's neutrality as agreed to in the treaty.
The costs of a neutrality breach are significant enough that the United
States may be justified in taking preemptive action.

The rhetoric, while appealing to nationalist sentiments, risks
undermining decades of diplomacy, established international law, and
US-Panama relations. However, [[Washington]{.underline}]{.mark}
[[cannot]{.underline}]{.mark} [afford]{.underline} [to]{.underline}
[[overlook]{.underline}]{.mark} [[China's]{.underline}]{.mark}
[growing]{.underline} [[influence]{.underline}]{.mark},
[[particularly]{.underline}]{.mark} [[given]{.underline}]{.mark} the
[[canal's]{.underline}]{.mark} strategic
[[importance]{.underline}]{.mark} [[in]{.underline}]{.mark}
[[the]{.underline}]{.mark} US-China
[[competition]{.underline}]{.mark}---China ranks as
its second-largest customer. Trump's statements likely aim to pressure
Panama on transit tariffs, caution Panama on increased reliance and
cooperation with China, and project US resolve. To protect its interests
without destabilizing the region, the United States must approach this
situation with strategic foresight and diplomatic precision.

#### The NWP AND NEP pose threats to the Panama and Suez canal's dominance

**Piecyk 21** (Adrian Piecyk, Field Team Captain at Etobicoke Centre,
Managing editor of the foreign observer, Doctor of Law from University
of Toronto, bachelor's in international relations, "A Bridge Across Two
Oceans: The Arctic Challenges To Panama Shipping Canal",
https://www.thearcticinstitute.org/bridge-across-two-oceans-arctic-challenge-panama-canal-shipping/,
// AS)

[[The Panama Canal's success depends on]{.mark} its
[convenience]{.mark}]{.underline} -- [[shorter distances equal
lower]{.mark} shipping [costs]{.mark}]{.underline}. The Canal
revolutionized international trade upon its opening, as it allowed
vessels to sail directly between the Atlantic and Pacific Oceans. [What
happens, then, when an even more convenient route opens, and seemingly
with no upfront cost?]{.underline} Just as the Panama Canal earns record
revenues year-after-year, [so too is [the Arctic Ocean seeing record
rates of melting ice]{.mark}]{.underline}. As of August 2020, the Arctic
Ocean maintains approximately 5.08 million km2 of thinner, late summer
ice coverage, but it has lost 3.15 million km2 of late summer ice since
1979, at a rate of close to 76,800km2 per year.6) [[The Northwest
Passage]{.mark}, a sea route which winds its way along Canada's Arctic
coast and [provides the shortest route to the east coast of]{.mark}
North [America]{.mark} from East Asia, could be ice-free and navigable
in summer within a matter of decades. Its sister, [the Northeast
Passage]{.mark}, chiefly follows the northern Russian coast, and so
[provides an alternative to]{.mark} shipping through [the
Suez]{.mark}]{.underline}. Between these two lies the more difficult but
direct Transpolar Route, which could circumvent many of the Northwest
Passage's navigational, depth, and territorial issues were its summer
ice to disappear, as could happen by 2050, according to scientific
predictions.7) The extent of thick, multi-year ice is down 60 percent
from 1980, and its replacement by thinner, seasonal ice could preclude
the need for ice breakers for Arctic shipping.8) A shorter, toll-free
transit route through the Arctic means not only less cost for freight,
but also reduced carbon emissions, and these factors could seriously
affect the decision-making of Panama's key transit partners. Arctic
Voyages -- Fluke or Trend? Over the past decade, [[several]{.mark}
high-profile [transits]{.mark} through the Northwest Passage
specifically [have]{.mark} [highlighted the danger of this]{.mark}
alternate [route to]{.mark} [the]{.mark} [Panama]{.mark}
[Canal's]{.mark} [dominance]{.mark}]{.underline}. Travelling from
Vancouver to Finland in 2013, [[the]{.mark} carrier [Nordic Orion saved
\$200,000]{.mark} and four days of sailing by taking the Northwest
Passage instead of the Panama Canal.]{.underline}9) More recently,
[[the]{.mark} Finnish [icebreaker]{.mark} [Nordica set a record]{.mark}
in 2017 [by travelling 10,000 km in]{.mark} a mere [24 days]{.mark} by a
similar route.]{.underline}10) Though these transits by individual ships
have a limited impact on a broader scale, [[they]{.mark} nevertheless
set a precedent which could [herald]{.mark} [far greater volumes of
traffic]{.mark} through the Arctic [in the]{.mark} near
[future]{.mark}.]{.underline}

#### That offers 50% savings through quicker deliveries

**Humpert 11**, Malte Humpert, 9/15/2011, Arctic Institute - Center for
Circumpolar Security Studies \"The Future of the Northern Sea Route\",
https://www.thearcticinstitute.org/future-northern-sea-route-golden-waterway-niche/JOW

The [[NSR]{.mark} represents a [**shortcut** for]{.mark} the [transfer
of goods]{.mark} between Europe and Asia [and]{.mark} thus
[offers]{.mark} **[significant cost savings]{.mark}** for shipping
companies]{.underline}. In theory[, [distance
savings]{.underline}]{.mark} [along the NSR can be as **[high as
50%]{.mark}** [compared]{.mark} to the currently used shipping lanes
[via **Suez or Panama**]{.mark}]{.underline}. Whereas a [[voyage]{.mark}
from Japan to Europe [take]{.mark}s roughly **[29 days]{.mark}** via the
Cape of Good Hope]{.underline} and 22 days via the Suez Canal[, it
**[takes just 10 days]{.mark}** [via]{.mark} **the [Arctic
Ocean]{.mark}**]{.underline}.2) The actual sailing distance between
Yokohama in Japan to Rotterdam in the Netherlands is roughly 20,000
kilometers passing through the Suez Canal, but less than 9,000
kilometers via the NSR.

#### 39% of profits shift

[Adrian
**Piecyk**](https://www.thearcticinstitute.org/author/adrian-piecyk/) ,
January 7, 20**21** -- Author at the Arctic Institute, "A Bridge Across
Two Oceans: The Arctic Challenge to Panama Canal Shipping", 2021,
https://www.thearcticinstitute.org/bridge-across-two-oceans-arctic-challenge-panama-canal-shipping/

Considering that [[the **N**]{.mark}**orth[w]{.mark}est
[P]{.mark}assage** [would]{.mark} best **[facilitate]{.mark}
[shipping]{.mark}** [between]{.mark} the US [East Coast and
Asia]{.mark},]{.underline} [[the]{.mark} [picture]{.mark}
[becomes]{.mark} even **[grimmer for Panama]{.mark}**.]{.underline} As
of 2019, [official stats record that a **staggering [39 percent]{.mark}
of total** tonnage [pass]{.mark}ing **[through]{.mark}** the
**[Panama]{.mark}** Canal travels along the East Coast-Asia
route]{.underline}, translating to 180,564,000 PC/UMS net tons out of
468,779,000 total.^11)^ [The next largest route by cargo throughput,
between the US East Coast and the west coast of South
America]{.underline}, comes out to 38,640,000 PC/UMS, or a mere 8
percent of total tonnage. The United States alone accounts for no less
than 66.4 percent of Canal traffic by origin and destination, followed
perhaps unsurprisingly by China at 13.6 percent.^12)^ [On a global
scale, [the Panama Canal]{.mark} [facilitates]{.mark} approximately [**5
percent of** total **oceanic shipping**]{.mark}, an impressive figure,
but one which is overwhelmingly dependent on the flow of trade between
the US and China.]{.underline}

Given the inexorable march of climate change and unprecedented ice melt
in the North, the question facing the Panama Canal in terms of future
traffic loss is less "if" and more "how much." [[The E]{.mark}ast [Coast
-- Asia route is]{.mark} indisputably [the cornerstone of Panama Canal
traffic]{.mark}, and [if the US ]{.mark}]{.underline}and China
[leap]{.mark} at the opportunity [[for]{.mark} up to 30 percent [shorter
journeys]{.mark} compared to taking the Canal,^13[)]{.mark}^[ the
**losses in revenue**]{.mark} for Panama [could be
**staggering**]{.mark}.]{.underline} Using the 2019 figures as a
baseline, a conservative loss of 25 percent along the East Coast -- Asia
route alone would see PC/UMS tonnage drop by 45,141,000, or more than
7,000,000 PC/UMS than the next leading route has total. A far worse
scenario with 75 percent losses would see a staggering drop of
135,423,000 PC/UMS, a whopping 29 percent of total tonnage.

### L \-- Icebreakers

#### Icebreakers necessary to opening investment opportunities in the Arctic

Megan **Drewniak et al. 21.** U.S. Coast Guard, Marine Safety Unit,
Toledo Ohio. "Ice-Breaking Fleets of the United States and Canada:
Assessing the Current State of Affairs and Future Plans." MDPI.
1-12-2021. https://www.mdpi.com/2071-1050/13/2/703 //reyrey edited for
clarity

The [number of vessels navigating these waters \[Northwestern Passage\]
is lower]{.underline} compared to the NSR. This is [due to]{.underline}
various reasons, including [complex geography]{.underline} (there are
many narrow and shallow corridors along this specific passage), as well
as ocean currents, [along with **drifting ice packs** that **block**
many of the entrance and exit site]{.underline}s. Also, there is a
different view between Canada and the U.S. regarding the legal regime of
the passage (Canada maintains the position that these waters are
"internal" therefore fall under its jurisdiction, but the U.S. maintains
the high seas status respectively) \[25,26\]. Hinterland connection is
another setback; venturing transport through the northern territories of
Canada and Alaska has been characterized as the logistical equivalent of
a lunar landing \[27\]. [[The NWP is mainly regarded as an
**alternative** to the Panama Canal]{.underline}]{.mark}, though the
investments on the latter to increase its size and crossing speed will
help maintain its competitiveness in the near future. [Additionally,
[**icebreaker services** in this area are extremely low]{.mark} both in
**quantity** and **quality** compared to those **offered by Russia** for
the **NSR**.]{.underline}

[In addition to navigation challenges and lack of infrastructure and
response capabilities, coupled with the potential of unforeseen maritime
accidents and environmental disasters]{.underline}, there are
[looming]{.underline} energy, infrastructure, and [geopolitical
ambitions amongst many countries]{.underline} with a vested interest in
the region (Figure 2). For many countries, [[the Arctic presents an
**investment opportunity** to elevate their strategic and **economic
significance** as well as boost their international
status]{.underline}]{.mark}. Russia, the United States, Canada, Denmark,
and Norway have all been trying to assert jurisdiction over part of the
Arctic. In fact, Denmark, Canada, Norway, and Russia have already made
submissions in relation to the Arctic seabed to the United Nations
Commission on the Limits of the Continental Shelf (CLCS), which was
established by the 1982 United Nations Convention on the Law of the Sea
\[28\]. Recently, in August of 2015, Russia resubmitted a bid to the
CLCS claiming vast territories in the Arctic, specifically laying claim
to 1.2 million square kilometers (km) of sea shelf extending more than
350 nautical miles from the shore \[29\].

Furthermore, [for these countries claiming a presence in Arctic
developments, it is **imperative** that a **prominent maritime
presence** is clearly established.]{.underline} It is important to fully
understand that [major technological advances]{.underline} in overall
safety systems onboard ships to [include design and
[construction]{.mark} standards]{.underline} and navigational aids and
equipment have **[[renewed]{.underline}]{.mark}** the [[interest
of]{.mark} both [Canada and the **U**]{.mark}nited **[S]{.mark}**tates
regarding the]{.underline} benefits associated with the
[NWP]{.underline} \[30\]. [[Canada maintains the]{.underline}]{.mark}
position that the [[NWP]{.underline}]{.mark}, especially those areas in
the Arctic Archipelago, [[are **internal**]{.mark} waters to Canada [and
should be provided with]{.mark} the [right to **prohibit**
transit]{.mark}]{.underline} \[23\]. [[This]{.mark} interpretation [is
**not held** by]{.mark} most [maritime nations]{.mark}]{.underline},
including the United States and a certain number of European Union's
member-states that consider the [[NWP as]{.mark} an
[international]{.mark} strait]{.underline} according to the description
outlined in the 1982 United Nations Law of the Sea Convention \[28\].
This distinction is incredibly important because applying the UNCLOS
provisions limits Canada's rights to enact fishing and environmental
regulations, fiscal and smuggling laws, shipping safety laws, but
[[leaves the passage]{.mark} as an **international** strait [open to
**all nations**]{.mark}]{.underline} \[31\].

#### Ice breakers are key to opening trade routes.

Robert **Hammitt 17**, Fellow at Polar Research and Policy Initiative,
researcher at Carleton University with a focus in Europe and Eurasian
studies, 1/24/27, "Icebreakers: an overview",
https://polarconnection.org/icebreakers/ //maeve

Whether owned and operated by national governments, state-owned
enterprises, or in private hands, some icebreakers and fleets are
primarily focused on commercial operations. [[Icebreakers have engaged
in many]{.mark} types of [commercial
activities]{.mark}]{.underline}[:]{.mark}

[escorting and [supporting cargo vessels engaged in Arctic
shipping]{.mark};]{.underline}

[[protecting oil and gas operations]{.mark} from ice;]{.underline}

[**[keeping ports and their approaches open to allow trade to
flow]{.mark}**.]{.underline}

In some sub-arctic waters, such as the Baltic Sea, seasonal
**[[commercial icebreaking is critical to enable trade through the
winter]{.underline}]{.mark}**. In addition, the Soviet and Russian
governments have always relied on the Northern Sea Route along Russia's
Arctic coast to bring natural resources out of Siberia and the far north
and to bring goods into those communities.

In more recent years, Russia has focused on expanding the Northern Sea
Route. This expansion is linked to increased mining and energy
extraction, especially on the Yamal Peninsula. Russia has also promoted
the Arctic route as an alternate global shipping route, offering a
considerably shorter voyage between northern Europe and East Asia than
the traditional Suez Canal route. However, to date, the particular
challenges and risks of this route have prevented its rapid growth.

[[Icebreakers are]{.mark} also [used to support]{.mark} oil- and
[natural gas]{.mark}-drilling and [exploration]{.mark}]{.underline}.
During its operations off the North Slope of Alaska, Shell leased the
privately-owned Aiviq and the MSV Fennica owned by Finland's Arctia
Offshore. In addition to protecting the exploration fleet from
unexpected ice conditions, icebreakers are necessary to provide access
to year-round production platforms, such as the Goliat platform and
Prirazlomnaya platform in Norwegian and Russian waters respectively.
Finally, [[icebreakers are an important point of oil-spill
response]{.underline}]{.mark}. Fennica was an important part of Shell's
Alaska operations because it carried a capping stack, necessary to close
a gushing well in the event of a blowout. Because US regulations for
Arctic drilling require that operators have the capacity to deal with a
blowout before the end of the season, a late-season disaster would force
response operations to continue even as winter ice returns.

Similarly, this fall's cruise through North America's Northwest Passage
by the cruise ship Crystal Serenity hired the RRS Ernest
Shackleton, operated by the British Antarctic Society, to escort it
through the Canadian Archipelago (the Shackleton is an ice-strengthened
ship for polar operations, rather than a true icebreaker). As with
Shell's icebreaker fleet, the Shackleton is intended to provide a safety
margin if severe ice conditions develop, rather than because Crystal
Cruises expects to travel through pack ice conditions.

As these examples demonstrate[, [commercial icebreaking operations are
driven by]{.mark} the [economic needs of shipping
companies]{.mark}]{.underline} and other maritime operators. This sets
an upper limit on the necessary upper icebreaking capacity, barring
emergency situations, since commercial operators are only willing to
face a certain degree of risk before their operations become
uneconomical. In addition, the world fleet of ice-strengthened
commercial ships, capable of safely travelling in heavier ice
conditions, is relatively small. Although more Polar-class ships will be
built, many operators, such as Russia's Norilsk Nickel, are looking
toward cargo ships with their own icebreaking capacity, able to operate
separately from an icebreaker-supported convoy. This tendency will not
entirely obviate the need for commercial icebreakers, but is a sign that
[[icebreaker-led convoys shouldn't be taken for granted as the form of
future Arctic shipping]{.mark}.]{.underline}

#### Arctic sea routes shift Chinese interest---Icebreakers key to navigate the region and prevent illiberal Arctic influence.

 [Gabriella
**Gricius**](https://www.thearcticinstitute.org/author/gabriella-gricius/),
3/18/20**21** -- PhD -Political Science, Colorado State University,
journalist for The Arctic Institute, "Geopolitical Implications of New
Arctic Shipping Lanes",
https://www.thearcticinstitute.org/geopolitical-implications-arctic-shipping-lanes/

[[States]{.mark} have [expressed]{.mark} [interest]{.mark} in using new
shipping lanes [for shorter transit times, strategic resource
extraction, military activity]{.mark} [as well as]{.mark}]{.underline}
regional shipping, fishing and tourism.^2)^ However, with its
unpredictable seas, severe climate conditions, high costs, and lack of
developed infrastructure in the northern territories -- the Arctic has
many obstacles for [[socio-economic]{.mark} and maritime
[development]{.mark}]{.underline}.^3)^

Despite these obstacles, [[this]{.underline}]{.mark} [renewed regional
interest [may lead to increased geopolitical
tensions]{.mark}.]{.underline} Increasing traffic brings the possibility
of unknown ships close to many of these country's coasts in a way that
they are not familiar with. [[States]{.mark} then must
[determine]{.mark} [how]{.mark} [to]{.mark} [control]{.mark} their
northern [borders]{.mark} [in harsh climates]{.mark} and territories
[that]{.mark}, until recently, [were]{.mark}
[impassable]{.mark}.]{.underline} Without some kind of vigilance over
their borders, Arctic states may also run the risk of facilitating or
falling victim to increased transnational crime. For example, unknown
ships also bring with them unknown purposes, of which many could be
illegal fishing or even illicit trafficking. [Further, [the development
of the NSR and NWP]{.mark} also [present]{.mark}s possible
[issues]{.mark}. [Unresolved]{.mark} [disputes]{.mark} [about]{.mark}
the [sovereignty]{.mark} [of]{.mark} these new [sea]{.mark}
[routes]{.mark} may be [problematic]{.mark} when shipping does
eventually become an option]{.underline}. [[China and Russia's
cooperation]{.mark} on the [development of the NSR]{.mark} also
[brings]{.mark} with it [concerns about Chinese influence in the
Arctic]{.mark}]{.underline} as well as [American
hand-wringing]{.underline} about a closer relationship between Russia
and China.

**The Northern Sea Route**

Although the Northern Sea Route (NSR) was first opened by the Soviet
Union in the 1930s, it has not been a reliable transit route for many
decades due to ice coverage.^4)^ However, with the increasing melting of
the polar ice caps, the NSR is looking more and more like a potential
transit route. However, there are geopolitical implications concerning
the NSR that raise interesting questions. Perhaps the most basic
geopolitical consideration is who has power over the NSR. Russia has
claimed that the NSR lies within its territorial waters, giving it
exclusive rights to develop the area and to patrol ships.^5)^ The United
States and other powers have disputed this claim, which has led to an
interesting paradox where neither side recognizes the veracity of who
can control this passageway. Interestingly, Canada does recognize
Russia's claim over the NSR as Russia recognizes Canada's claim over the
NWP (*See below*).

Another geopolitical concern is the development of the NSR. While the
United States has failed for many years to express interest, not all
states have taken such an approach. [China, for example, is working with
Russia to develop the NSR as [China sees the route as]{.mark} [important
for its national economic interests]{.mark}]{.underline}. Although the
investment is hugely expensive and time consuming[, [China]{.mark}
clearly sees the [development]{.mark} of the NSR as [playing the long
game]{.mark}.]{.underline}^6)^ While the route may not be usable now, in
a few decades, that could easily change. [[Geopolitically]{.mark},
[this]{.mark} has huge implications as it [brings China and
Russia]{.mark} closer together [as allies and encourages both states to
develop more icebreakers]{.mark} -- a [necessary]{.mark} tool [to
navigate the region.]{.mark}]{.underline}

### L\-\--Greenland

#### Cooperation with Greenland is key to access shipping routes.

**Hiar** **and Northey**, 20**25** \[Corbin; Award-winning environmental

United States officials have for more than a century viewed Greenland's
vast deposits of oil, gas and critical minerals as a treasure chest
that's waiting to be opened, government documents show.

But the challenge of unlocking those riches from the rugged, ice-covered
island consistently has stymied the development efforts of ambitious
mining firms and some of the world's largest oil companies.

[Now, President Donald [Trump]{.mark} is reviving an old idea with a new
geopolitical twist: To [prevent greater Chinese control of the
Arctic]{.mark} and rare earth minerals market, the U.S. would take over
the Danish territory --- by buying or seizing it.]{.underline} Trump's
strong-arm tactics toward Denmark have sent shock waves through Europe
and the North Atlantic Treaty Organization defense alliance, while
industry analysts have been left scratching their heads.

#### Infrastructure key to displacing Panama's dominance in the shipping industry

**Piecyk 21** (Adrian Piecyk, Field Team Captain at Etobicoke Centre,

of Toronto, bachelor's in international relations, "A Bridge Across Two

[Finally, [the]{.mark} last and seemingly greatest [obstacle to
an]{.mark} [Arctic]{.mark} [overthrow of the]{.mark} Panama
[Canal's]{.mark} [dominance is its]{.mark} remoteness and the
[difficulty]{.mark} inherent [in developing]{.mark} the
region.]{.underline} As early as 2005, [the US Arctic Research
Commission noted that even with melting ice, [a lack of natural
harbours, transport or commercial infrastructure,]{.mark} and especially
bathymetric and topographic data of possible routes would seriously
[hamper large-scale trade]{.mark} through the
region.]{.underline}34[) It is easy to say that development in the
Arctic would be unnecessary]{.underline} when the ultimate destination
of goods flowing through the North is Europe or the American East Coast,
but this fails to consider the importance of the "Pendulum Model" of
maritime shipping, where vessels make several stops along their journeys
to pick up and drop off cargo.35) This model requires not only
significant port infrastructure, but also large enough markets to make
such deliveries profitable [-- [two things]{.mark} [the Arctic]{.mark}
notably [lacks]{.mark}, and [which the ACP recognizes as a]{.mark}
[benefit]{.mark} [for the Canal]{.mark}.]{.underline}36) [[The Canadian
North]{.mark}, where much of the Northwest Passage flows, [has]{.mark}
only [one]{.mark} single [developed]{.mark} deepwater [port]{.mark} in
Churchill, Manitoba, [and]{.mark} [it]{.mark} not only [lacks]{.mark}
[rail]{.mark} [connections]{.mark} to the rest of
Canada]{.underline},37), [but [melting]{.mark} [permafrost puts]{.mark}
both [existing structures and]{.mark} future [development on dangerous
ground]{.mark}.]{.underline}38)

[[Large scale development]{.mark} of the Arctic for shipping [is]{.mark}
further [hampered by the concerns of Indigenous groups for the effects
this could have]{.mark} on their land and water]{.underline}. Though the
Polar Code is attempting to limit possible environmental damage, [its
seeming failure to stop the use of heavy fuel oil bodes ill for how
effective it can be in minimizing pollution]{.underline}. Nevertheless,
the Inuit Circumpolar Council has not hesitated to assert its land
claims in the face of both foreign and domestic encroachment.39) This is
especially relevant as melting ice puts ever greater pressure on
Indigenous communities' food security and vital ice roads, which are
already threatened by climate change.40)

#### Infrastructure investment key to unlocking economic potential of the Arctic

Megan **Drewniak et al. 21.** U.S. Coast Guard, Marine Safety Unit,

### L ---Military

#### Militarizing the Arctic secures control of Arcitc shipping routes.

**Basak**, 20**24** \[Ashton; international political economy major,
fellow at the Georgetown Security Studies Review; "The Arctic Trilemma:
The United States Must Compete for the Transpolar Sea Route" Georgetown
Security Studies Review; 10/5;
https://georgetownsecuritystudiesreview.org/2024/10/05/the-arctic-trilemma-the-united-states-must-compete-for-the-transpolar-sea-route/\]
JX

The Threat Landscape

While the TSR may not fully open for another 20 years, [[Russia has
already taken steps to **cement its influence in the
region.**]{.underline}]{.mark} [In July 2022, Russian President Vladimir
Putin declared the Russian military will defend its claims to the Arctic
"by all means" necessary.]{.underline} Putin has backed his rhetoric
with substantive commitment to the area: [Russia has about 33% more
military bases in the Arctic Circle than NATO does.]{.underline} While
this may be unsurprising because 53% of the Arctic coastline is under
Russian control, that does not mean that the United States and NATO
partners should accept a position of regional inferiority.

Russia has more icebreakers -- valuable military assets that help clear
icy paths for commercial and military ships -- than NATO. The U.S. only
accounts for two icebreakers in NATO's undersized fleet, lagging far
behind its adversary. Icebreakers will be essential in making use of the
TSR as early as the 2030s. This may seem relatively far away. But the
race begins now. Icebreakers need to be constructed. Military bases need
to be built. Transit infrastructure needs to be set up. All of this
takes time, [and for the U.S. to be ready when the Arctic becomes
accessible, **it must lay the groundwork now.**]{.underline}

[Given Russia's military bases and icebreakers, scholars believe that
the West trails 10 years behind Russia militarily in the region. [Russia
has established a foothold near the CAO that will give it
oversight,]{.mark} control and area-denial capabilities [over the
TSR]{.mark}. The U.S. must contest the aspiring Russian hegemony in
order to better position the United States toward active use of the
TSR.]{.underline}

The potential for Sino-Russian collaboration in the region is
concerning. In 2018, China claimed to be a "near-Arctic state,"
signaling that it seeks to shape the political and economic future of
the region. Beijing's goal for a Polar Silk Road that connects Russia to
the Belt and Road trade initiative has led to billions of dollars in
foreign direct investment. Even more concerning is the aggressive
rhetoric with which the Chinese military frames its regional
aspirations. Beijing emphasizes that the Arctic represents a "scramble
for new strategic spaces," and thus, China "cannot rule out the
possibility of using force." This is particularly relevant to the TSR:
in the summer of 2020, the Chinese icebreaker MV Xue Long 2 voyaged the
TSR for the first time. It would be a mistake to think that Russia is
the only adversary to U.S. economic interests in the TSR.

The Arctic Trilemma: The United States' Way Forward

Given the regional and strategic complexities outlined above, what
should the U.S. policy be, and why? To recall, the Arctic trilemma has
three legs: [access to the TSR, a greater U.S. military presence in the
region, and stability and cooperation vis-a-vis Russia.]{.underline} One
may consider each side of the trilemma. [First, [if the U.S. wants free
economic access]{.mark} to the TSR but wants to avoid a hostile economic
environment by cooperating with Russian interests , [it must sacrifice
**establishing a greater military presence in the region.**]{.mark}
Russia will not unconditionally cooperate and promote an uncontested
peace if the U.S. military challenges their positions in the
region.]{.underline} Putin's invasion of Ukraine provides telling
evidence: the authoritarian has asserted that he can and will respond
forcefully to perceived U.S.and NATO threats to Russian sovereignty. As
a result, the U.S. would have to concede to Russian oversight to ensure
cooperation and economic access, a position which Washington should --
and would -- never accept.

Figure 2: The Arctic Trilemma for the United States

[Alternatively, [the U.S. **can have primary access**]{.mark} **to the
TSR [and advance military objectives in the region]{.mark}** [,]{.mark}
but sacrifice cooperation with Russia and prompt Great Power Competition
in the Arctic. The [Russia]{.mark}ns already [have an entrenched
military presence in the Arctic and can leverage its assets to
dominate]{.mark} the TSR if they are uncontested.]{.underline} [Thus,
[the United States]{.mark} must [**cultivate a formidable military force
in the region** to ensure that U.S.companies can utilize the TSR to
their maximum economic advantage. Open sea lanes would have to be
secured through U.S. and Allied sea control.]{.mark} The competition to
be the economic hegemon of the TSR makes any opportunity for joint
efforts toward mutual prosperity unlikely and military steadfastness a
necessity.]{.underline}

Lastly, the U.S. can maintain peace and project a strong military
footprint but suffer from suboptimal access to the TSR. The political
climate of the Arctic would resemble a Cold War-esque gridlock due to
military build-up by both nations and potentially others who wish to
enter the mix. The militarization of the Arctic would sacrifice the
greater economic gains both sides could have achieved through a
demilitarized strait..

Within this trilemma, the U.S. must secure access to the TSR and
establish a strong military presence in the Arctic, embracing great
power competition. Given Putin's militarism and completely shattered
relations with the West post-February 2022, Washington should not pursue
cooperation. To secure U.S.economic interests in the TSR in the 2040s is
to ensure no adversary will threaten U.S. ships. Naval theorist Alfred
Thayer Mahan argued that securing access to the high seas for trade and
commerce is crucial for a nation. Sea lines like the TSR are the
economic arteries of Great Powers. They sustain economic life. And they
are too important to be left under adversarial control. The U.S. must
command the Arctic sea, like it has commanded the Pacific and other
oceans for the better part of half a century

Conclusions

[[U.S. dominance of the TSR]{.mark} is **valuable for economic reasons
because it reduces [trade time and cost.]{.mark}**]{.underline} Yet, in
a Mahanian fashion, [TSR [economic gains are contingent on]{.mark}
United States and Russian [military competition in the Arctic]{.mark}
Circle.]{.underline} Confronted with a trilemma, [[the U]{.mark}nited
[S]{.mark}tates [must pursue]{.mark} two of three policy goals:
[economic access]{.mark} to the TSR [and greater military
presence]{.mark} around the route.]{.underline} Cooperation with Russia
will only lead to feeble U.S. economic and defense posture in the
region. [Russia has a clear economic interest in accessing a faster
trade route in the TSR and its clear military buildup of the Arctic has
forced the U.S. hand to match it with its own military
might.]{.underline} The U.S. military can do this by making changes on
the strategic, operational, and tactical levels by expanding its number
of Arctic bases, shifting jurisdiction of the Arctic to USEUCOM, and
placing ESBs in the GSN Gap. The United States. must establish itself as
a regional power in the Arctic to realize TSR's economic potential
before it opens in the 2040s.

### L\-\--Navigation/Surveying of waters

#### [Navigational]{.underline} aids increase Arctic shipping.

Ekaterina **Uryupova '24** \[April 30, 2024; Dr. Ekaterina Uryupova is a

Climate change alters [**ship's** itineraries]{.underline} in the
**[North]{.underline}**

About [**[10]{.mark}** [percent]{.mark}]{.underline} of [Arctic
[waters]{.mark} are [**surveyed** to modern]{.mark}]{.underline} or
adequate **[[standards]{.underline}]{.mark}**, and appropriate
[corrections]{.underline} for charts [must be applied]{.underline}
according to data sources and alterations originating from climate
change processes.13) When it comes to natural factors, such as weather
and climate, the [lack of **technology**]{.underline}, [insufficient
**infrastructure**]{.underline} and equipment, and [human
factors]{.underline} may significantly [[decrease]{.mark} the
[**safety** of **navigation**]{.mark} across the Arctic]{.underline}.14)

The frequency of extreme weather events is growing in the North. These
changes are affecting the Arctic shipping in many ways, including the
increase in risk for navigation. Lower central pressures for the
poleward-shifted storms, which are increasing in number, may result in
increased wind speeds and extreme wave heights in polar regions, with an
associated increase in the sea-spray icing of ships.15) For example,
this was a reason why the fishing vessel Onega capsized in the Barents
Sea in December 2020, after a period of extreme sea-spray icing
conditions.16)

Out-of-date [nautical **[charts]{.mark}** may pose [a **huge
risk**]{.mark} for navigation]{.underline} in shallow waters and fjords
of the Arctic. Concerns should be raised in the maritime [**industry**
over the **quality** of the data]{.underline} upon which the charts are
based, since the age and [accuracy]{.underline} of data on nautical
charts may [significantly vary]{.underline}. One of the reasons for the
inaccuracy of data might be an [environmental **shift** caused by
climate **change**]{.underline}. In fjords with active melting glaciers
-- a meltout sediment from the basal ice [adds **sediment** to the
**flow** and increases the deposition rate]{.underline}, and as a result
the [bottom **topography** changes **significantly** over the
years]{.underline}.17) These factors were likely the case when the
expedition cruise ship The Ocean Explorer got stuck in the mud in a
remote area of Alpefjord, East Greenland, in 2023.18) Numerous examples
show that the harsh environment of polar regions still may pose a
considerable risk to ship navigation.

The Polar Code: Sailing through a plethora of challenges

The Polar Code,the international code of safety for ships operating in
polar waters, is a relatively recent and significant milestone in
international regulation of navigation in polar waters,which covers a
wide range of requirements to ships operating in the frigid waters of
the Arctic Ocean and adjacent seas.19) It has been created as a
compulsory framework for safe ship operation and environmental
protection in icy waters. On one hand, the Polar Code's provision gives
a solid universal set of operating standards for various vessels sailing
in the polar areas. On the other hand, experts identify multiple notable
gaps in the documents, especially related to the environmental
protection, pollution, and safety of various types of vessels.20) Among
other obstacles, issues related to climate change are not properly
covered in the Code at all. A broad variety of climate-induced
environmental impacts includes not only temperature and sea-ice
conditions mentioned in the documents but many other parameters such as
fog, sea-spray icing, atmospheric rivers, unusual storms, and more.

The Polar Code has been improving since the time it was adopted by the
International Maritime Organization (IMO) in 2014. The most significant
modifications are related to pollution of the Arctic Ocean, such as the
prohibition on the use of heavy fuel,21) and discussions on underwater
noise pollution.22) However, it is [[difficult to find]{.mark} any
**[advice]{.mark}** [on]{.mark} the [**prediction** and
**mitigation**]{.mark}]{.underline} of [hazardous weather events caused
by climate change]{.underline} on [ships]{.underline} operating in polar
regions. Icy [waters remain highly **unpredictable** for various types
of **ships**]{.underline}, even the best prepared and equipped, as
recent incidents demonstrate.

As a result, significant [[efforts]{.mark} [should be made to
**research**]{.mark}]{.underline}, **[[develop]{.underline}]{.mark}**
and [[improve]{.mark} life-saving **equipment**]{.underline} and
[[communication]{.underline}]{.mark} technology aboard the vessels
operating in the polar waters.23) The need for independent maritime
regulatory authorities as well as the development of state transport
policies, and wider [[information-sharing]{.mark} **relating** to the
**analysis** of the impact of climate change]{.underline} should be
[taken into **account**]{.underline}.24)

Conclusion

The polar regions can still be considered among the [most
**challenging** environments to **navigate** by **sea**]{.underline} due
to [risks]{.underline} presented [by unpredictable **weather** and
**climate** factors]{.underline}. The [number of **[ships]{.mark}**
[sailing in Arctic]{.mark} waters]{.underline} is
**[[increasing]{.underline}]{.mark}** and will [continue to
**[grow]{.mark}** over coming decades]{.underline}, which will
[[produce]{.mark} both **[opportunities]{.mark}** and
**challenges**]{.underline}. The escalating impacts of climate change in
the northern waters have created [ample [**opportunities**
for]{.mark}]{.underline} Arctic **[[shipping]{.underline}]{.mark}**
activities [[related to cargo]{.mark} shipping]{.underline},
[fishing]{.underline}, expedition cruise tourism, [mineral resource
**exploration**]{.underline}, science, and naval activities. Visual
observation using satellite images can help in [selecting
**[safer]{.mark}** [routes]{.mark} to [avoid]{.mark}
**[icebergs]{.mark}**]{.underline} and sea [ice]{.underline}. However,
extreme weather events and the [effects of climate change]{.underline}
such as Arctic [[cyclones]{.underline}]{.mark}, unexpectedly high
[[ocean **waves**]{.underline}]{.mark}, and rapid [sea ice
[drift]{.mark} may increase **uncertainties** in forecasts]{.underline}
and [make **navigation** unsafe]{.underline}. Well-established
[communication]{.underline} and
[information-**[sharing]{.mark}**]{.underline}
[[between]{.underline}]{.mark} researchers and [[ship
crews]{.underline}]{.mark} are [[crucial]{.mark} [for]{.mark} all
kinds]{.underline} of **[[vessels]{.underline}]{.mark}** to make
[information **complete**]{.underline}, [precise]{.underline}, and
[available]{.underline}. In addition, [together with international
maritime **laws** and **policies**]{.underline} and the Polar Code, a
development of a shared governance framework to cope with the various
challenges stemming from the climate change impact will be crucial in
the future.

### L ---REM

#### U.S. rare earth mining in Greenland [trades-off]{.underline} with focus on the Panama Canal for [new]{.underline} Arctic shipping routes.

**Milman '25** {Oliver; 1/13/25; Milman is an environmental
correspondent for the Guardian. He wrote the acclaimed The Insect Crisis
in 2022 'It's ironic': how climate crisis is driving Trump push on
Greenland and Panama'
https://www.theguardian.com/environment/2025/jan/13/trump-greenland-panama-canal-climate-crisis?}

Donald Trump's desire to seize control of Greenland and the Panama canal
is being shaped in part by a force that he has sought to deny even
exists -- the climate crisis.

Last week, [Trump ramped up his **demands** that the United States
**annex** both **Greenland** and the Panama canal,]{.underline} refusing
to rule out economic or even military interventions to take them and
threatening "very high" tariffs upon Denmark, of which Greenland is an
autonomous territory, if it opposes him.

["We need them for **economic security**," [Trump said]{.mark}. "The
**Panama Canal** is vital to our country, it's being operated by
**China**. China! We gave the Panama canal to Panama, not China." The US
president-elect added that [**Greenland** was required for "national
**security** purposes" and]{.mark} that [Denmark "should give it
up"]{.mark}.]{.underline}

What do Greenlanders make of Donald Trump's advances for their home?

Trump's rhetoric has been denounced by other world leaders but the
rationale for this expansionism is being influenced, experts say, by
something affecting both Greenland and Panama -- rising global
temperatures caused by the burning of fossil fuels.

Even though the incoming US president has called climate change a "giant
hoax", his son [[**Donald Jr** acknowledged the value of
**mining**]{.mark} **[r]{.mark}**ar**[e]{.mark}**
**[m]{.mark}**ineral**[s]{.mark}** [in Greenland]{.mark} that are being
uncovered as the ice rapidly retreats from the vast Arctic
island]{.underline}. Greenland's enormous ice sheet is losing an average
of 30m tonnes of ice an hour due to the climate crisis, raising sea
levels and potentially collapsing vital ocean currents.

[Donald Jr said on a trip to the island last week that he wanted to
"make Greenland great again", accusing Denmark of blocking its
self-governing territory from developing "the great **natural
resources** that they have]{.underline}, whether that's coal, whether
that's uranium, whether that's other [rare minerals]{.underline},
whether that's gold or diamonds".

[As sea ice dwindles in the Arctic Ocean, meanwhile, [new **shipping
routes**]{.mark}]{.underline} [through the far northern latitudes are
[becoming more **viable**]{.mark}. Robert **O'Brien**, [Trump's former
national security adviser]{.mark}, [said that Greenland]{.mark}, which
has had a US military base since 1941, is key to counter the threat of
China and Russia but it [is]{.mark} also ["very important to the
Arctic]{.mark}, [which is]{.mark} going to be the critical battleground
of the future because as the climate gets warmer, the **Arctic** is
[going to be a pathway that]{.mark} maybe [**cuts down** on the usage of
the **Panama canal**]{.mark}".]{.underline}

While global heating is causing Greenland to shed its ice, in Panama it
has helped spur a severe drought that has gripped the country since
2023. This drought has caused the human-made Gatún Lake, which supplies
the water for the canal, to plummet by several feet, limiting traffic
through the famed shipping thoroughfare.

Last year, shipping entering the canal slumped by nearly a third due to
these restrictions. The US reasserting dominion over the Panama canal,
which it handed over to Panama via a treaty in 1999, would, like in
Greenland, give it opportunistic control over resources increasingly
strained by the climate crisis.

["**[Greenland]{.mark}** has lost massive amounts of ice, making it more
[attractive for **rare earth mining**]{.mark} and oil drilling, while we
are [already seeing more **traffic** through the Arctic Ocean]{.mark} as
it becomes ice free for longer," said Alice Hill, a former climate
adviser to Barack Obama and now a fellow at the Council on Foreign
Relations.]{.underline}

"In Panama, climate change impacts how the canal operates and puts
pressure on the US to find different routes or try to get priority over
China for the canal itself.

"Climate change is altering the fundamental calculus of the strategic
importance of the Arctic as well as the Panama canal," Hill added. "It's
ironic that we are getting a president who famously called climate
change a hoax but is now expressing interest in taking over areas
gaining greater importance because of climate change."

The impacts of a superheated planet are helping refashion geopolitics in
a variety of ways, as droughts and storms cause people to migrate,
conflicts erupt over resources such as water and borders are even
redrawn between some countries as snow and ice dwindle.

[The US isn't the only power attempting to capitalize upon the upending
of a stable climate, with China hatching plans for a "polar silk road"
that will connect Chinese ports to Europe and beyond via a northern
shipping route as the Arctic becomes less dominated by ice.]{.underline}

"Climate change is shaping geopolitics even if leaders don't want to
admit it," said Sherri Goodman, an author and expert on the polar region
at the Wilson Center. "China is clear-eyed about the climate threat and
they will take advantage of that in access to resources and
infrastructure. We ignore this climate threat at our peril.

["[Trump]{.mark}, I think, [sees]{.mark} this [rush for **resources**
and climate change is making them more
**accessible**]{.mark}.]{.underline} It's interesting to see how
isolationist he was in his first term, now he seems almost imperialist.
It's hard to know where this will end up."

This scramble for resources, and the rise of nationalist leaders in
several countries including the US, has stoked fears of a sort of
rightwing environmentalism taking hold where wealthier countries trample
over those in vulnerable nations as climate disasters escalate.
Jean-Noël Barrot, France's foreign minister, warned last week: "We have
entered an era that is seeing the return of the law of the strongest."

Leaders in Greenland, Denmark and Panama have all rejected Trump's calls
for US assimilation. It's a sentiment many people living in these
places, often overlooked in the geopolitical wrangling, share. "Our
country is ours -- it's not for sale," said Ole Hjorth, 27-year-old air
traffic controller from Nuuk, Greenland. "Trump says a lot of things
that aren't very serious, but now it's become quite scary."

"We don't want to be American and these threats are really pushing
people in Greenland away from the Trump administration and the US," said
Aaja Chemnitz Larsen, a Greenlandic politician who represents one of the
two seats that Greenland has in Denmark's parliament. "It's been quite
disrespectful how this has been handled."

Chemnitz Larsen, a supporter of Greenlandic independence from Denmark,
said there was [**enthusiasm** for new **economic
opportunities**]{.underline}, such as tourism and [even **rare earth
mining**]{.underline}, to diversify from traditional practices such as
fishing but that the climate crisis was a stark reality in realizing
this.

"Climate change is affecting everything in Greenland, we are seeing land
becoming exposed and also new species in the sea," she said. "For us,
climate change is real, there's no doubt that it's here and will become
more difficult for us in time."

### L -- Trade Routes

#### The Northwest Passage becomes the new norm.

Jill **Kimball '22** \[June 22, 2022; Jill is an experienced
communication professional with a background in higher education, arts
administration and journalism. "Melting Arctic ice could transform
international shipping routes, study finds," Brown;
https://www.brown.edu/news/2022-06-22/arctic\] \$G

Another critical consequence of melting ice in the Arctic? The
[**potential** for **[shorter]{.mark}**]{.underline}, [more
[eco-friendly]{.mark} maritime **[trade]{.mark}** routes]{.underline}
that [[bypass]{.underline}]{.mark} the Russian-controlled [[Northern Sea
**Route**]{.underline}.]{.mark}

In a new study, a pair of climate scientists at Brown University worked
with a legal scholar at the University of Maine School of Law to predict
how Arctic Ocean ice melt could affect the regulation of shipping routes
over the next few decades. They projected that by 2065, the Arctic's
[navigability will **[increase]{.mark}** so greatly that it could
**yield** new [trade **routes**]{.mark} in **international**
waters]{.underline} --- not only [reducing the shipping **industry's**
carbon **footprint** but also **weakening** Russia's **control** over
**trade**]{.underline} in the Arctic.

The study was published on Monday, June 20, in the Proceedings of the
National Academy of Sciences.

"There's no scenario in which melting ice in the Arctic is good news,"
said Amanda Lynch, the study's lead author and a professor of Earth,
environmental and planetary sciences at Brown. "But the unfortunate
reality is that the [ice is already **retreating**]{.underline}, these
[[routes]{.mark} are [**opening** up]{.mark}]{.underline}, and we [need
to start thinking **critically**]{.underline} about the legal,
environmental and geopolitical implications."

Lynch, who has studied climate change in the Arctic for nearly 30 years,
said that as a first step, she worked with Xueke Li, a postdoctoral
research associate at the Institute at Brown for Environment and
Society, to model four navigation scenarios based on four likely
outcomes of global actions to halt climate change in the coming years.
Their projections showed that unless global leaders successfully
constrain warming to 1.5 degrees Celsius over the next 43 years, climate
change will likely open up several new routes through international
waters by the middle of this century.

One critical consequence of melting ice in the Arctic: the [potential
for **shorter**]{.underline}, [more eco-friendly **maritime**
trade]{.underline} routes that [bypass th]{.underline}e
Russian-controlled [Northern Sea **Route**]{.underline}, shown here in
green.

According to Charles Norchi --- director of the Center for Oceans and
Coastal Law at Maine Law, a visiting scholar at Brown's Watson Institute
for International and Public Affairs, and one of the study's co-authors
--- those changes could have [major **implications** for world **trade**
and global **politics**]{.underline}.

Norchi explained that since 1982, the United Nations Convention on the
Law of the Sea has given Arctic coastal states [enhanced **authority**
over primary **shipping** routes]{.underline}. Article 234 of the
convention states that in the name of "the [prevention]{.underline},
**[reduction]{.underline}** and [control of marine pollution from
vessels]{.underline}," **[countries]{.underline}** whose coastlines are
near Arctic shipping routes have the [ability to **regulate** the
route's maritime **traffic**]{.underline}, so long as the area remains
ice-covered for the majority of the year.

Norchi said that for decades, [Russia]{.underline} has used Article 234
[for its own **economic** and geopolitical **interests**]{.underline}.
One Russian law requires all vessels passing through the Northern Sea
Route to be piloted by Russians. The country also [requires that passing
vessels pay **tolls** and provide advance **notice**]{.underline} of
their [plans]{.underline} to use the route. The heavy regulation is one
among many reasons [why major **shipping** companies often **bypass**
the route's heavy **regulations**]{.underline} and [high
costs]{.underline} and instead [use the **Suez** and **Panama**
canals]{.underline} --- [longer]{.underline}, [but cheaper and
easier]{.underline}, trade routes.

But as the [[ice]{.underline}]{.mark} near Russia's northern coast
[begins to **[melt]{.mark}**]{.underline}, Norchi said, [[so
will]{.mark} the [**country's** grip on **shipping**]{.mark} through the
Arctic Ocean]{.underline}.

"The Russians will, I\'m sure, continue to invoke Article 234, which
they will attempt to back up with their might," Norchi said. "But they
will be challenged by the international community, because Article 234
will cease to be applicable if there\'s no ice covered-area for most of
the year. Not only that, but with melting ice, shipping will move out of
Russian territorial waters and into international waters. If that
happens, Russia can't do much, because the outcome is driven by climate
change and shipping economics."

#### Arctic trade [revolutionizes]{.underline} supply chains and ensures [global]{.underline} resilience.

Jill **Kimball '22** \[June 22, 2022; Jill is an experienced
communication professional with a background in higher education, arts
administration and journalism. "Melting Arctic ice could transform
international shipping routes, study finds," Brown;
https://www.brown.edu/news/2022-06-22/arctic\] \$G

Another critical consequence of melting ice in the Arctic? The
[**potential** for **[shorter]{.mark}**]{.underline}, [more
[eco-friendly]{.mark} maritime **[trade]{.mark}** routes]{.underline}
that [[bypass]{.underline}]{.mark} the Russian-controlled [[Northern Sea
**Route**]{.underline}.]{.mark}

In a new study, a pair of climate scientists at Brown University worked
with a legal scholar at the University of Maine School of Law to predict
how Arctic Ocean ice melt could affect the regulation of shipping routes
over the next few decades. They projected that by 2065, the Arctic's
[navigability will **[increase]{.mark}** so greatly that it could
**yield** new [trade **routes**]{.mark} in **international**
waters]{.underline} --- not only [reducing the shipping **industry's**
carbon **footprint** but also **weakening** Russia's **control** over
**trade**]{.underline} in the Arctic.

The study was published on Monday, June 20, in the Proceedings of the
National Academy of Sciences.

"There's no scenario in which melting ice in the Arctic is good news,"
said Amanda Lynch, the study's lead author and a professor of Earth,
environmental and planetary sciences at Brown. "But the unfortunate
reality is that the [ice is already **retreating**]{.underline}, these
[[routes]{.mark} are [**opening** up]{.mark}]{.underline}, and we [need
to start thinking **critically**]{.underline} about the legal,
environmental and geopolitical implications."

Lynch, who has studied climate change in the Arctic for nearly 30 years,
said that as a first step, she worked with Xueke Li, a postdoctoral
research associate at the Institute at Brown for Environment and
Society, to model four navigation scenarios based on four likely
outcomes of global actions to halt climate change in the coming years.
Their projections showed that unless global leaders successfully
constrain warming to 1.5 degrees Celsius over the next 43 years, climate
change will likely open up several new routes through international
waters by the middle of this century.

#### **Arctic sea routes** undermines the Panama Canal and reshapes global shipping patterns. **Guo et al. '22.** Guo, Jianke, Shu Guo, and Jing Lv. \"Potential spatial effects of opening Arctic shipping routes on the shipping network of ports between China and Europe.\" *Marine Policy* 136 (2022): 104885. https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/abs/pii/S0308597X21004966

According to statistics from the United Nations Conference on Trade and
Development (UNCTAD), total maritime trade was 11.08 billion tons in
2019, with liner shipping carrying more than 80% of the world\'s total
merchandise trade \[2\], confirming that [[**maritime** transport plays
a crucial **role** in]{.mark} international trade in the context of
[**supply chain** globalization]{.mark}]{.underline}. Mainlane
East--West containerized trade routes, namely Asia--Europe, one of the
busiest liner routes in the global shipping network, handled 23%
worldwide containerized trade flows in 2019 \[2\]. Connecting the
Mediterranean Sea to the Red Sea, the Suez Canal Route lies at the
crossroads of Europe, Asia and Africa and is the most important route
between Asia and Europe. Twelve per cent of the world\'s cargo and 30
per cent of container trade is transported through the Suez Canal.
According to the Suez Canal Authority(SCA), nearly 19,000 ships passed
through the canal in 2020, an average of around 50 per day. The Suez
Canal has already been significantly affected by the surge in trade. The
grounding of a giant Panamanian cargo ship named Ever Given in March
2021 got stuck in the Suez Canal for six days in March, it dammed up
worldwide shipping and froze nearly \$10 billion in trade a day. For the
sea route from Asia to Europe, liner companies usually choose the Suez
Canal instead of the Cape of Good Hope when travelling between the Asian
and European markets. The reason for this is that a detour through the
Cape of Good Hope in South Africa would add approximately 10,000
kilometres to the voyage and cost an additional US\$470,000. With this
event, the advantage of the Suez Canal will be diminished when
considering the cost of congestion \[3\] and a new trade route between
the Asian and European markets will be the focus of attention.

[According to the monitoring and forecasting of sea ice melting
conditions in the Arctic performed by the NSIDC and NASA, it is believed
that [by 2035]{.mark}, sea **[ice]{.mark}** in the Arctic [will
have]{.mark} completely [**disappeared** and year-round
navigation]{.mark} in the Arctic will have [become a
**reality**]{.mark}. The accelerated [melting]{.mark} of Arctic sea ice
has greatly [increased]{.mark} the possibility of [establish]{.mark}ing
the [ASR]{.mark}. If the Arctic route is normalised, it would be one of
the [most convenient channels]{.mark} connecting Europe, Asia, and the
United States, as well as the Pacific, Atlantic, and Arctic Oceans,
[and]{.mark} would [revolutionise]{.mark} the world maritime [trade
pattern]{.mark} for the third time, [after]{.mark} the Suez Canal and
**[Panama Canal]{.mark}**]{.underline} \[4\]. The so-called Arctic route
is a combination of three shipping lanes: the Northeast Passage, which
starts from northern Europe and crosses the northern coastal region of
Russia to the east; the Northwest Passage, which starts from the Bering
Strait and crosses Canada and the United States to the east; and the
cross-polar route, which theoretically crosses the central region of the
Arctic Ocean directly to the Greenland Sea (Fig. 1).

With the concept of the 'Silk Road on Ice', the [[Arctic]{.mark} has
become a [focus]{.mark} of domestic and international
[attention]{.mark}]{.underline}. According to NASA satellite data, the
ASR was first opened in 2007. Then, in 2009, the successful voyage of
the German ships 'Friendship' and 'Vision' from Korea to the port of
Rotterdam marked the beginning of the commercial operation of the ASR
\[5\]. The following year, a cargo ship of the Norwegian shipping
company Chudi reached China via the **Northeast** Passage \[6\]. In
2013, the COSCO Shipping vessel 'Yongsheng' became the first Chinese
merchant vessel to take the Arctic route. From 2013--2017, COSCO
Shipping sent 10 vessels to the Northeast Passage of the Arctic route
and performed 14 voyages in total[. As of 2018, COSCO Shipping had
completed 22 voyages in the Northeast Passage of the Arctic, with a
total delivery volume of 619,981 ton]{.underline}s. According to Russian
statistics, the total cargo volume through the Arctic Ocean route in
2017 reached 9.7 million tons; 615 voyages passed through the Arctic
Ocean from the beginning of 2018 to July 15, 2018. In addition, the
Russian government approved numerous ships to use only the ASR and plans
to reach 80 million tons of currency per year in the ASR by 2040. [The
[trial]{.mark} [operation]{.mark} [of]{.mark} many countries in [Europe,
the United States, and Asia]{.mark} in the ASR has [accelerated]{.mark}
the **[normalisation]{.mark}** of Arctic route operation [and]{.mark}
further [promoted]{.mark} the [opening of the]{.mark} Arctic
[route]{.mark}]{.underline} (Fig. 2).

With the trial operation of various shipping companies in the Arctic,
the normalisation of the ASR has accelerated[. As a short and
economically viable route, the normalisation of the ASR will invariably
[affect]{.mark} the [traditional shipping structure]{.mark} and will
[change]{.mark} the flow direction of the world shipping organisation,
in addition to reshaping the spatial pattern of [shipping geography,
international trade, and world energy supply]{.mark}]{.underline}.

#### Engagement is [zero-sum]{.underline} -- [China's power]{.underline} depends on [America's neglect]{.underline}. 

**Kreps and Flores-Macías 13** --- Sarah E. Kreps & Gustavo A.
Flores-Macías, Assistant Professors of Government at Cornell ("No
Strings Attached? Evaluating China's Trade Relations Abroad," The
Diplomat, 5/17/2013, Available Online:
<http://thediplomat.com/china-power/no-strings-attached-evaluating-chinas-trade-relations-abroad/>,

To be sure, China may not have a purposeful plan to bring their trade
partners into alignment on foreign policy questions. [Even if
unintentional]{.underline}, however, this [**"gravitational effect**"
has a sound economic basis.]{.underline} Developing countries in Africa
and Latin America are comparatively much more dependent on China than
China is on these countries. In a ten year period, for example, Sudan's
trade with China rose from 1 to 10% of its Gross Domestic Product. That
pattern is even starker in a country like Angola, for which trade with
China represented 25% of its GDP in 2006. While China certainly needs
access to the resources in these countries, the individual countries are
far less important to China than China is to these countries. The
asymmetry in needs gives China a bargaining advantage that translates
into foreign policy outcomes even if not by explicit design. Whether by
design or not, the convergence with China's foreign policy goals is
important on at least two levels. First, developing countries in Africa
and Latin America may be lulled by the prospect of partnering with a
country such as China that does not have an explicit political agenda,
as did the United States and Soviet Union during the Cold War, but this
appears to be an illusion. Whether this reaches the level of "new
colonialism" as former Secretary of State Hillary Clinton referred to it
remains to be seen, but the economic asymmetries that undergird the
relationship make that prospect more likely. A second set of
implications deals with the United States[. [During the same period in
which China's trade with]{.underline}]{.mark} Africa and **[[Latin
America]{.underline}]{.mark}** and foreign policy convergence [have
[increased, the United States and China have actually
diverged]{.mark}]{.underline} in their overall UNGA voting behavior.
[[This suggests something of a **zero sum** dynamic in which China's
growing trade relations make it easier to attract allies in
international forums while US influence is
diminishing]{.underline}.]{.mark} Taken together, these trends call for
greater engagement on behalf of the United States in the developing
world. [Since]{.underline} the September [2001]{.underline} attacks,
[[Washington has dealt with]{.underline}]{.mark} Africa and [[Latin
America through **benign neglect**]{.mark} and shifted its attention
elsewhere. If foreign policy alignment does follow from tighter
commercial relations, the US ought to reinvigorate its trade and
diplomatic agenda as an important means of projecting influence
abroad.]{.underline}

The history of Greenland

The Kingdom of Denmark began colonizing Greenland in the early 18th
century, hundreds of years after Vikings from the same distant land
first arrived to set up residency. It was not until World War II that
the U.S. established a presence on the island, when then-Danish
Ambassador to the U.S., Henrik Kauffmann, refused to surrender to the
rule of Denmark\'s Nazi occupiers.

Denmark was liberated from Nazi occupation in 1945, but the U.S. did not
leave its military base, Pituffik Space Base, which to this day remains
the American military\'s northernmost installation.

The history of the Panama Canal

[The Panama [Canal]{.mark} was [built by]{.mark} the [U.S]{.mark}.
between 1904 and 1914]{.underline}. It serves as a shortcut between the
Pacific Ocean and the Caribbean, dramatically shortening shipping routes
from Asia to ports in the eastern U.S.

[It was first [managed by the U.S. government]{.mark} for decades, which
caused tension with Panama.]{.underline}

In the 1970s, the U.S. and Panama signed a treaty agreeing to the
permanent neutrality of the canal. [The U.S. committed to relinquishing
control of the canal and did so fully in 1999. The canal is now run by
the Panama Canal Authority.]{.underline}

#### No pivot in the status quo \-\--

#### A \-- Lacks shipping reliability

**Humpert** **11**, Malte Humpert, 9/15/2011, Arctic Institute - Center
for Circumpolar Security Studies \"The Future of the Northern Sea

https://www.thearcticinstitute.org/future-northern-sea-route-golden-waterway-niche/JOW

The majority of [cargo [ships]{.mark} that ply the world's oceans
[operate]{.mark} on **[regular schedules]{.mark}**]{.underline}, called
[liner service]{.underline}. In total more than 6,000 ships, most of
them container ships, [[follow]{.mark} a set [route]{.mark} calling at a
number of ports to load and unload cargo]{.underline}. [The global
[maritime industry operate]{.mark}s [on]{.mark}
**[just-in-time]{.mark}** cargo [deliveries]{.mark}. The [ability to

[guarantee uninterrupted]{.mark} service [are key]{.mark} for container

## Internals

### Turns Case

#### Chinese influence is a financial and military conflict multiplier

**Lansing institute 25,** Robert Lansing Institute, 1-21-2025, institute
that provides non governmental fact based research for non profit,
\"U.S. Strategic Interests and Potential Intervention in Panama: Risks
and Probabilities\",
https://lansinginstitute.org/2025/01/21/u-s-strategic-interests-and-potential-intervention-in-panama-risks-and-probabilities/
JOW

[[China's interest]{.mark} in the Panama Canal [poses]{.mark} several
potential [threats]{.mark} to U.S. strategic, economic, and geopolitical
interests. The Panama [Canal is]{.mark} a critical [chokepoint]{.mark}
for global trade and a symbol of U.S. influence in the Western
Hemisphere. [Increased]{.mark} Chinese [involvement]{.mark} could
[undermine U.S. dominance]{.mark} in the region.]{.underline} Here are
the primary concerns:

---

1\. Strategic Control Over a Key Maritime Chokepoint

-- [[Disruption of U.S. Military and Trade Routes]{.underline}]{.mark}:
The Panama Canal is vital for U.S. military mobility, particularly for
transferring naval assets between the Atlantic and Pacific Oceans. If
China gains significant control or influence over canal operations, it
could potentially disrupt U.S. strategic movements.

-- [Leveraging Control During Conflicts]{.underline}: In a geopolitical
or military standoff, Chinese control could theoretically be used to
block or delay U.S. access to the canal, impacting both military and
commercial operations.

---

2\. Economic Risks to U.S. Trade

-- Influence Over Global Trade Routes: The Panama Canal is crucial for
U.S. trade, especially with Asia. Chinese influence could result in
preferential treatment for Chinese vessels or goods, potentially
disadvantaging U.S. shipping companies and increasing costs.

-- [[Economic Leverag]{.mark}e]{.underline}: By investing in canal
operations or infrastructure, China could use its position to influence
trade terms or extract concessions from the U.S. and other countries
dependent on the canal.

3\. Expansion of Chinese Geopolitical Influence

-- [[Undermining U.S. Influence in Latin America]{.underline}]{.mark}:
Increased Chinese involvement in Panama reflects Beijing's broader
strategy to expand its influence in Latin America, traditionally seen as
the U.S.'s sphere of influence. This challenges U.S. dominance and
erodes its diplomatic leverage in the region.

-- [Belt and Road Initiative]{.underline} (BRI): China's engagement in
Panama is part of its global BRI strategy, aimed at establishing trade
and infrastructure networks that connect China with key markets. By
integrating Panama into this network, China strengthens its foothold in
the Western Hemisphere.

---

4\. [[Potential Espionage and Cybersecurity Risks]{.underline}]{.mark}

-- [Data and Communications Vulnerabilities]{.underline}: If Chinese
companies gain control of port facilities, logistics hubs, or
telecommunications infrastructure linked to the canal, it could provide
opportunities for espionage or data interception.

-- [[Surveillance Risks]{.underline}]{.mark}: Infrastructure projects
often involve advanced technology systems, which could be used for
monitoring or surveillance activities in the region.

5\. Economic Dependency on China

-- Debt Diplomacy: Panama could become economically dependent on China
through large-scale infrastructure investments tied to the canal. This
could give Beijing undue influence over Panama's policies, potentially
aligning them with Chinese interests rather than U.S. priorities.

-- [[Erosion of Sovereignty]{.underline}]{.mark}: Chinese investment
often comes with strings attached, potentially compromising Panama's
ability to maintain neutral or pro-U.S. policies.

6\. Strategic Partnerships with Regional Rivals

-- [[Military Presence Concerns]{.underline}:]{.mark} While China does
not currently have a military presence in Panama, its investments could
pave the way for future military or quasi-military activities, such as
port access for Chinese naval vessels.

-- [Collaboration with Adversaries]{.underline}: Increased Chinese
presence in Panama could facilitate coordination with other U.S. rivals,
such as Russia, in undermining U.S. influence in the Americas.

---

7\. [Undermining U.S.-Panama Relations]{.underline}

-- Diplomatic Shifts: By establishing deeper economic ties with Panama,
China could weaken Panama's traditionally strong ties with the U.S.,
making it less likely to align with U.S. positions on global issues.

-- [[Economic Competition]{.underline}]{.mark}: Chinese investments
could outpace U.S. efforts in Panama, reducing American companies'
opportunities and influence in the region.

[China's growing [interest]{.mark} in the Panama Canal
[represents]{.mark} a [multifaceted challenge]{.mark} for the
U.S.,]{.underline} encompassing strategic, economic, and geopolitical
dimensions. [[To counter]{.mark} these threats, the [U.S.]{.mark} must
[strengthen]{.mark} its [presence in Panama]{.mark}, deepen economic and
diplomatic ties, and collaborate with regional partners [to
ensure]{.mark} the canal remains a [neutral and secure]{.mark}
asset]{.underline}. Balancing competition with China while safeguarding
U.S. Interests will require sustained attention and strategic action.

### IL -- Climate

#### Chinese control of Latin America pushes warming past the tipping point.

**Edwards**, 20**15** \[Guy; research fellow and co-director of the

https://dialogue.earth/en/climate/1508-is-china-pulling-latin-america-in-an-unsustainable-direction/\]

For example, [there is remarkable potential for China and CELAC to

CELAC.]{.underline} The conditions in Latin America and the Caribbean

[The newly created China-CELAC Forum has the potential to be a

Forum as a means to engage with China on taking action on climate change
within their bilateral partnerships and ensure that Chinese-Latin

ambitious and equitable global climate agreement.

[The economic slowdown in Latin America and the Caribbean and China is
no excuse to undermine environmental or climate policies to attract
foreign investments for short terms gains]{.underline}. Instead [it
presents an opportunity to set Latin America and the Caribbean on the
path towards creating low-emission and resilient economies.]{.underline}
To that end, the China-CELAC Forum could prove pivotal to Latin American
and Caribbean countries' efforts to respond to climate change.
