### IL -- Dual Use

#### Chinese dual-use influence in Latin America decimates missile defense.

**Yang**, **6/11** \[Domingo; PhD in diplomacy from National Chengchi
University; assistant research fellow at the Institute for National
Defense and Security Research; "The Overlooked Front in Missile Defense:
China's Ground Stations in Latin America" Sinopsis; 6/11/2025;
https://sinopsis.cz/en/the-overlooked-front-in-missile-defense-chinas-ground-stations-in-latin-america/\]
jx

The Trump administration is advancing the Golden Dome missile defense
initiative with a clear eye on the growing threat posed by China's
hypersonic weapons capabilities. Integrated with space technologies, the
Golden Dome is envisioned as a layered architecture for sensing,
tracking, and intercepting a range of aerial threats---including
ballistic, cruise, and hypersonic missiles---from adversaries.

China's deployment of hypersonic glide vehicles (HGVs) presents a
formidable challenge to U.S. homeland security, propelled by advances in
thermal management, delivery platforms, and space-based support systems.
These HGVs can be launched from China's intercontinental ballistic
missiles---such as the DF-5, DF-31, and DF-41---capable of striking
targets anywhere within the continental United States.

While experts continue to debate the Golden Dome's projected \$175
billion price tag and raise concerns over its funding and operational
viability, [this analysis contends that any credible effort to
[shield\[ing\] the U.S.]{.mark} homeland [from China's long-range
missile threat must]{.mark} also [address]{.mark} the **expanding
footprint of [Chinese ground stations across Latin
America.]{.mark}**]{.underline}

[Latin America: **A critical frontier for Chinese ground
stations**]{.underline}

A comparison of active joint satellite initiatives and ground station
deployments suggests that [China places greater strategic emphasis on
establishing ground stations in Latin America.]{.underline} As of 2025,
China and regional partners jointly operate approximately four active
satellites---primarily for remote sensing and communications---including
two CBERS-4 satellites with Brazil, the VRSS-2 with Venezuela, and
TKSAT-1 with Bolivia. In contrast, China maintains at least eight ground
stations across the region, with facilities in Argentina (Neuquén and
Río Gallegos), Bolivia (Amachuma and La Guardia), Venezuela (El Sombrero
and Luepa), as well as in Chile (Cerro Calán), and Brazil (Alcântara).
Although China is not directly involved in the ground stations located
in Iquitos, Lima, Piura, Pucallpa, Puerto Maldonado, and Tacna, Peru
nonetheless maintains significant space cooperation with Beijing through
multilateral initiatives and satellite data-sharing agreements.

[China also plans to invest \$80 million in a major astronomical
observation base at Cerro Ventarrones, Chile, featuring an Extremely
Large Telescope and aiming for completion by 2028.]{.underline} However,
the project was suspended in March 2025 amid security concerns over its
sensitive location and the involvement of China State Construction
Engineering Corporation---[a firm designated by the U.S. as a **"Chinese
military company"---as the main contractor.**]{.underline}

[Ground stations facilitate **long-range precision strike
capability**]{.underline}

[[Ground stations are **central to China's remote sensing
architecture,** enabling real-time operations, reducing data latency,
and extending geospatial coverage.]{.mark} These assets [**enhance
China's situational awareness** over the U.S. homeland and **bolster the
PLA's kill chain effectiveness**]{.mark}: find, fix, track, target,
engage, and assess.]{.underline} [Recent Chinese military
activities---including hypersonic glide vehicle tests in 2021 and 2023,
and an intercontinental ballistic missile launch into the Pacific in
2024---underscore the [expanding reach of China's remote sensing
network, with ground stations serving as **its critical
backbone.**]{.mark}]{.underline}

China's deep space ground station in Neuquén, Argentina, underscores its
expanding technical reach. During the return of the Chang'e-5 (CE-5)
lunar probe on December 17, 2020, the station played a critical role in
tracking the mission's complex "skip reentry" maneuver---filling a key
gap in China's overseas Very Long Baseline Interferometry (VLBI)
network. The telemetry data it provided was essential in calculating the
probe's reentry trajectory. This same high-precision orbit determination
capability could support the positioning of HGVs equipped with sensors
and advanced guidance algorithms---underlining how the return of CE-5
also validated the strategic potential of China's hypersonic precision
strike capabilities against the United States.

China's Expanding Dual-Use Influence in Latin America

[Recent analyses increasingly suggest that China's Belt and Road
Initiative [(BRI) serves]{.mark} not only economic purposes but also
[strategic and military objectives.]{.mark} Many BRI
[projects]{.mark}---ports, airports, and digital infrastructures---[are
dual-use by design and located at geopolitical chokepoints,]{.mark}
**extending the PLA's global reach.**]{.underline} **[[China's
infrastructure projects in Latin America are no
exception.]{.underline}]{.mark}**

China's Chancay mega port in Peru---financed by COSCO
Shipping---embodies the dual-use risks of BRI infrastructure. Marketed
as a commercial hub, its deep-water berths and advanced cranes could
support PLA Navy logistics near the Panama Canal. Similar concerns
surround Brazil's Paranaguá port and Ecuador's Manta port, which are
controlled by China Merchants Port Holdings and Hutchison Port Holdings
respectively, and operate under opaque agreements that raise red flags
over potential intelligence gathering and covert transfers.

A key player in China's BRI, [the PLA-linked and heavily subsidized
Huawei has deeply embedded itself in Latin America's digital
infrastructure.]{.underline} Offering below-market bids, it now supplies
70% of the region's 4G networks and leads in 5G deployment. Huawei also
operates major cloud data centers in Chile, Mexico, and Brazil, and has
built three critical submarine cables---South Atlantic Inter Link, Fiber
Optic Austral, and Topolobampo--La Paz---through its affiliate, HMN
Technologies.

In addition to ports and telecoms, [China is investing heavily in Latin
America's energy infrastructure.]{.underline} In Chile, state-owned
firms State Grid Corporation and China Southern Power Grid now control
70% of the national transmission network. [This energy leverage could
strengthen Beijing's hand in negotiations over the suspended Cerro
Ventarrones ground station project. More broadly, [secure access to
logistics, data transmission, and electricity enhances the performance
of China's ground stations and remote sensing systems---**sharpening its
situational awareness near the U.S.** ]{.mark}**homeland.**]{.underline}

**[[The overlooked front in missile defense]{.underline}]{.mark}**

[China's [ground stations]{.mark}, remote sensing capabilities, and
dual-use infrastructure are [turn]{.mark}ing [L]{.mark}atin
[A]{.mark}merica [into an overlooked front in U.S. missile
defense.]{.mark}]{.underline} As debate continues over the cost and
effectiveness of the Golden Dome, [[Washington must]{.mark} **rebalance
its priorities** and **[confront China's growing]{.mark} space and
[dual-use infrastructure]{.mark} footprint [in its own
hemisphere.]{.mark}**]{.underline}

[By proactively engaging in the Latin America's evolving space and
infrastructure landscape, Washington can **safeguard its homeland
security interests, counter covert threats,** and **ensure that the
Western Hemisphere remains a domain of open cooperation** rather than
opaque competition.]{.underline}

#### That risks nuclear escalation.

**Brown**, 20**21** \[Gerald; analyst with Valiant Integrated Services
focusing on nuclear deterrence and East Asian security; "Understanding
the Risks and Realities of China's Nuclear Forces" Arms Control
Association; June;
https://www.armscontrol.org/act/2021-06/features/understanding-risks-and-realities-chinas-nuclear-forces\]
jx

[War Control and Inadvertent Escalation]{.underline}

There is little evidence that technological entanglement is a direct,
strategic choice, but there are some limited indications that [[China
could use nuclear signaling to constrain the extent of conventional
conflicts]{.mark} and contribute to escalation control]{.underline}.25
Nuclear signaling includes such actions as test launches, release of the
locations of targets, an increase in readiness levels, missile
deployments, or other actions to demonstrate resolve. The goal would not
be necessarily to use nuclear weapons. Instead, the signaling would aim
to raise fears that a conflict could credibly escalate to the nuclear
level, thus "causing the enemy to dread that the possible consequences
of its actions will be that its losses will exceed its gains, thereby
causing the enemy to change its plans for risky activities and achieving
the goal of restricting the war to a certain scope."26 In this way,
[[China could **capitalize on the uncertainty of a potential nuclear
conflict**]{.mark} to deter intervention and constrain escalation in
conventional conflicts in the Pacific region. Such risks are compounded
by China's use of purposeful ambiguity as an integral component of its
approach to nuclear deterrence.]{.underline}27

**[One major problem is that [such signaling by the Chinese may be
indistinguishable from preparations for a nuclear
attack.]{.mark}]{.underline}** Yet, [[writings]{.mark} by experts on
deterrence and signaling operations f[ail to acknowledge that these
provocative actions could be misinterpreted by an
adversary.]{.mark}]{.underline} In general[, [Chinese experts seem to
believe that nuclear escalation is unlikely to be effectively
controlled]{.underline}]{.mark}[,]{.underline} but are overconfident
that conventional conflict can be controlled without escalating to the
nuclear level.28 [[Lack of awareness about escalation risks could very
well make the **PLA more aggressive in local

The [Panama Canal: A Vital Economic and Strategic Asset]{.underline}

Facilitating [6% of global maritime trade, [the]{.mark} Panama [Canal is

**Atlantic**]{.mark} Oceans. [This efficiency enhances U.S. trade

Historical Context: The Handover and Its Implications

In 1999, the Panama Canal transitioned to Panamanian control under the
Torrijos-Carter Treaties, negotiated during President Jimmy Carter's
administration. While celebrated as a diplomatic milestone, the transfer
has remained controversial among some U.S. policymakers, who view it as
a strategic misstep.

Trump's recent remarks highlight lingering concerns over this decision.
On his Truth Social platform, he described the canal as a "vital
national asset," emphasizing its [importance to the U.S. **economy** and
**security**.]{.underline} Trump's rhetoric includes a threat to demand
the canal's return if American interests are not adequately safeguarded.

Economic and Strategic Concerns

Central to Trump's critique is the [high toll fees imposed on American
shipping and the canal's strategic management.]{.underline} Beyond
economic considerations, [broader [anxieties about **China's** regional
**influence**]{.mark} have [amplified]{.mark} these
[concerns]{.mark}.]{.underline} Since Panama's 2017 diplomatic
recognition of China over Taiwan, [[Beijing has **expanded**]{.mark} its
[**presence** in the region]{.mark}]{.underline}, investing heavily in
infrastructure projects near the canal.

[**[China]{.mark}**'s influence includes [**control** over **ports** at
both ends of the canal]{.mark}]{.underline} through Hutchison Ports PPC,
a Hong Kong-based company with ties to Beijing. This control has sparked
fears about potential manipulation of canal operations, threatening the
neutrality enshrined in the Neutrality Treaty. U.S. Southern Command has
also voiced concerns, warning that Chinese investments could compromise
the canal's impartiality and security.

Security Implications

In addition to logistical control, there are fears that [China [could
**embed** **surveillance** technology within the canal's
infrastructure]{.mark}, [enabling **espionage** against U.S.
**naval**]{.mark} and **commercial** [operations]{.mark}.]{.underline}
[This potential surveillance capability [poses significant
**risks**]{.mark}, [providing]{.mark} critical [**insights** into U.S.
**logistics** and military movements.]{.mark}]{.underline}

Challenges to Reclaiming Control

Reclaiming the Panama Canal would face significant legal and diplomatic
hurdles. The Neutrality Treaty, which guarantees the canal's impartial
operation, obligates the United States to respect Panamanian
sovereignty. Any unilateral move to regain control would breach
international law and strain U.S. relations in Latin America.

Moreover, Panama has demonstrated effective management of the canal,
including a 2016 expansion that doubled its capacity to accommodate
larger vessels. Despite these advancements, Trump's statements
underscore the canal's enduring importance to U.S. interests and the
need for vigilance in ensuring secure and reliable access.

Balancing Geopolitical Dynamics

As global trade routes evolve, the Panama Canal remains a focal point of
U.S. foreign policy. Navigating the interplay of American strategic
interests, Panamanian sovereignty, and China's regional influence will
require a nuanced approach. [Maintaining the canal's **neutrality** and
operational **efficiency** is essential for safeguarding supply chain
**stability** and international **commerce**.]{.underline}

In this era of shifting geopolitical landscapes, [the [Panama
Canal]{.mark}'s role as a critical maritime route underscores its
[continued **relevance** to global **trade** and U.S. national
**security**.]{.mark}]{.underline}

#### The Panama Canal is [key]{.underline} to global trade\-\--the US must take the lead against China.

**Tandon 25** \[Vaibhav; 3/21/25; Chief International Economist at the
Northern Trust Corporation, Msc in international business, International
Economics from Lancaster University; Northern Trust, "The Importance of
the Panama Canal,"
https://www.northerntrust.com/united-states/insights-research/2025/weekly-economic-commentary/the-importance-of-the-panama-canal\]

[[Panama]{.underline}]{.mark} is a small country that [[plays a big role
in **global commerce**]{.underline}]{.mark}. The country\'s most iconic
attraction is [[the **Panama Canal**]{.underline}]{.mark}, the
engineering marvel that [[has become]{.mark} a [**flash point**
for]{.mark} global [trade **tensions**]{.mark}]{.underline}.

The Canal is a highly strategic 51-mile waterway that connects the
Atlantic and Pacific Oceans.  [[It enables ships]{.mark} to
avoid]{.underline} the [**lengthy** and **hazardous** route around Cape
Horn]{.underline}, thereby [**dramatically** cutting down shipping
times]{.underline}. 

[[The waterway accounts for]{.mark} almost [**6%** of global maritime
trade]{.mark}]{.underline}, servicing 144 maritime routes [used by more
than **150** nations.  Around **14,000** ships pass]{.underline} through
the canal [each year, [carrying over 500 million tons of **food**,
**minerals** and **factory-made** products]{.mark}]{.underline}.  About
[**[\$270 billion]{.mark}** in cargo is handled
[annually]{.mark}]{.underline}.

[[The **U**]{.underline}]{.mark}nited **[[S]{.underline}]{.mark}**tates
[[is the **largest user**]{.mark} of the Panama Canal.  About
[**three-quarters** of traffic]{.mark}]{.underline} passing through the
isthmus [either departed from or [is]{.mark} heading to [U.S.
ports]{.mark}]{.underline}.  Trade between Asia and the U.S. east coast
accounts for 42% of the Canal's total traffic.

[[China]{.mark} and Hong Kong'[s influence in]{.mark} global maritime
[supply chains has been **growing**]{.mark}]{.underline}, as companies
from those countries hold significant stakes in key shipping
infrastructure like the Panama Canal.  [A [Hong-Kong]{.mark}-based
corporation [controls **two** of]{.mark} the [four major
ports]{.mark}]{.underline} surrounding the waterway; [[this is]{.mark}
perceived by the]{.underline} new [U.S.]{.underline} Administration [as
[a]{.mark} potential [**threat** to American
interests]{.mark}]{.underline}. Pressure has been applied to reduce this
perceived security risk.

As a result, a Hong Kong-based company recently struck an agreement to
sell shares of its facilities to a consortium led by a major American
firm for close to \$23 billion.  But the deal has drawn the ire of
Chinese authorities.

High transit tariffs have also become a focal point for the Trump
administration.  It has alleged that canal fees violate the [Permanent
Neutrality
Treaty](https://pancanal.com/wp-content/uploads/2022/07/neutrality-treaty.pdf),
which requires transit fees to be "just, reasonable, equitable and
consistent with international law." 

Operators of the port note that two years of record drought led to a
major reduction in water levels in the Gatun Lake, the reservoir that
supplies the canal.  This forced the authorities to implement
restrictions on ship traffic, leading to record-high transit fees,
longer transit times and disruptions to global trade.  In late 2023,
only 22 ships crossed the canal each day instead of the usual 36.

Beijing had extended its national security laws to Hong Kong in 2020. 
This is also a concern for the U.S. Under the laws, mainland authorities
can seek information from Hong Kong companies for monitoring shipping
and the strategic positions of naval vessels.

A [[conflict over]{.mark} control of [the]{.mark} Panama [Canal would be
**disastrous** to]{.mark} international [supply
chains]{.mark}]{.underline}.  A [[triumph]{.mark} of engineering [is at

**frictions**]{.mark}]{.underline}.

#### **The Canal [supercharges]{.underline} trade\-\--the US must beat China for influence in ports.**

**Posadas '25** \[Daphne; 2/9/25; Associate Editorial Director at the
Foundation for Economic Education, MA in Economics from Troy University;
FEE, "The Panama Canal: An Artery for Global Trade,"
<https://fee.org/articles/the-panama-canal-an-artery-for-global-trade/>\]

In his inaugural address, Donald Trump declared that one of his
administration's [priorities](https://apnews.com/article/rubio-trump-panama-canal-greenland-cf950b07453035d3e25d23807bc136e8) would
be to regain control of the Panama Canal. This claim reopens a
century-old debate over who built it, who lost it, and who truly owns
this 80-kilometer engineering marvel that stitches together the Atlantic
and Pacific Oceans. [With over **140** maritime routes, **1,700** ports,
and **160** countries linked through it, [the Canal is a
**key [artery](https://pancanal.com/canal-de-panama-es-conectividad/#:~:text=Por%20eso%2C%20el%20Canal%20de,a%20la%20prosperidad%20de%20Panam%C3%A1.)** of
global trade]{.mark}]{.underline}.

I visited the Canal a few years ago, and, beyond its logistical genius,
what struck me the most was its turbulent history.

French Vision, American Execution, Caribbean Workers

In 1869, fresh off his success with the Suez Canal, French diplomat
Ferdinand de Lesseps set his sights on Central America. He was
determined to carve a passage through the Isthmus of Darien, which was
then part of Colombia.

This would be one of the 19th century's most ambitious infrastructure
projects.

A [report](https://www.economist.com/the-americas/1879/07/26/the-panama-canal) in The
Economist from 1879 called it "a bolder enterprise even than the Suez
Canal," predicting the logistical, technical, and health nightmares
ahead. As if they had a crystal ball, the project collapsed just a
decade later, sunk by financial ruin, yellow fever, and an engineering
failure. Lesseps's Panama Canal Company would be bankrupt by 1888.

Yet where the French had failed, the United States saw an opportunity.
Invoking the [Monroe
Doctrine](https://www.britannica.com/event/Monroe-Doctrine)---a US
foreign policy that justified American influence in Latin America while
keeping the continent off-limits to European colonial
ambitions---Washington backed Panama's independence from Colombia in
1903. It then secured the Hay-Bunau-Varilla Treaty granting it full
control over the Canal's construction and operation. Nearly [40,000
workers](https://bdigital.binal.ac.pa/bdp/tomoXXIXP3.pdf) from the
Caribbean---mostly from Barbados---were recruited to build the Canal.
And in August 1914, just as World War I erupted, the Panama Canal
opened, redrawing the map of global trade.

[Without the Canal, global shipping routes would be **significantly**
different]{.underline}. Before 1914, ships traveling the east and west
coasts of the American Continent faced a long and dangerous journey.
[[The Canal slashed **fuel consumption**, cut **transit times**, and
made]{.mark} global [trade more **efficient**]{.mark} than
ever]{.underline}.

Today, a San-Francisco--New-York voyage has [five possible
routes](https://sea-distances.org/) by sea. The fastest? Through the
Panama Canal---10 days, 23 hours. The next-best option? A 27-day detour
around the Strait of Magellan.

The Canal Moves the World... But at What Cost?

For much of the 20th century, the Panama Canal was a [geopolitical chess
piece](https://www.pbs.org/newshour/world/panama-canal-helped-make-u-s-world-power).
The Canal Zone was US territory and a flashpoint for anticolonial
sentiment in Central America. By the 1960s and '70s, Panamanian
resentment escalated, culminating in the 1964 Flag Riots. Clashes
between Panamanians and US forces resulted in multiple casualties.

In 1977, the Torrijos-Carter Treaties established a phased transition
plan that transferred control of the Canal to Panama by 1999, marking a
major shift in US-Panama relations. Trump has called this handover
"foolish," blaming President Jimmy Carter for giving away a trade
goldmine. At the time, the decision was intended to reduce anti-American
sentiment in Latin America.

Today, [[the]{.mark} Panama [Canal is a **vital** economic
force]{.mark}]{.underline}. Managed by a private entity, it generates
roughly 4% of Panama's GDP, through the tolls paid by vessels using the
Canal. [About [**5%** of global trade flows through it]{.mark}
annually]{.underline}, and [[the US is]{.mark} its biggest
user---[around **40%** of]{.mark}]{.underline} US container
[[traffic]{.underline}]{.mark} passes through each year.

Meanwhile, [[[China](https://www.bbc.com/mundo/articles/c3vp9rw5rero) is
**catching up**]{.mark} fast]{.underline}. From October 2023 to
September 2024, [China accounted for **21.4%** of]{.underline} the
[cargo]{.underline} volume transiting the Canal---[[making it]{.mark}
the **[second largest]{.mark}** user]{.underline} after the United
States. [[Beijing has]{.underline}]{.mark} also [[been **investing** in
Panamanian ports]{.mark}, raising concerns over]{.underline} who has
more [influence]{.underline}.

And the stakes are rising...

[Water
shortages](https://www.economist.com/the-americas/2024/01/09/the-dwindling-of-the-panama-canal-boosts-rival-trade-routes) in
Lake Gatun (one of the lakes that feed the Canal) restrict ship
crossings and raise questions about the Canal's long-term viability, as
does the fact that the century-old locks are not big enough to
accommodate today's largest container ships. All the while, Nicaragua is
reviving dreams of an alternative Canal, backed by Chinese investors.

Trade Is Fragile

It's easy to take global trade for granted---until it breaks. In 2021,
one of the world's largest container ships, the Ever Given, [got
stuck](https://fee.org/articles/that-ship-that-blocked-the-suez-did-what-tariffs-do-every-day/) in
the Suez Canal for six days, freezing \$10 billion in global trade per
day.

The lesson? Apart from countless memes, the Ever Given crisis reminded
the world that maritime chokepoints matter.

Accidents are not the only threat to trade. Recent Houthi rebel attacks
in the Red Sea---where 15% of global trade crosses---have forced
shipping companies away from Suez, rerouting around Africa, adding weeks
to delivery and millions in extra cost---proving just how vulnerable
global trade is.

[[The]{.mark} Panama [Canal's **strategic**
infrastructure]{.mark}]{.underline} has [[fueled
**prosperity**]{.underline}]{.mark} in a country that stands out among
its peers, [proving]{.underline} that [commerce **drives**
progress]{.underline}. But as history shows, when politicians meddle in
trade, they distort it. Washington once controlled the Canal for
geopolitical leverage, and [[now Beijing seeks **influence**]{.mark}
over its ports]{.underline}. But [the]{.underline} real [power behind
the Canal has]{.underline} never been politics---it has [always been
**free trade**]{.underline}.

#### Democratic backsliding kills economic growth.

**Mosley '23** \[Layna; 7/31/23; Professor at Princeton University,
Director of Princeton Sovereign Finance Lab; Harvard Law School Forum on
Corporate Governance, "The Financial and Economic Dangers of Democratic
Backsliding,"
https://corpgov.law.harvard.edu/2023/07/31/the-financial-and-economic-dangers-of-democratic-backsliding/\]

The [[last decade]{.underline}]{.mark} has
[[witnessed]{.underline}]{.mark} the [[**erosion**
of]{.mark} [[democratic **institutions**]{.mark} and
**practices**](https://www.science.org/doi/10.1126/science.abb2434) in a
wide range of countries]{.underline}, including established democracies,
newer democracies, and countries thought to be on the path from
autocracy to democracy. In its 2023 report, the V-Dem Institute [details
this
trend](https://v-dem.net/documents/30/V-dem_democracyreport2023_highres.pdf),
noting that, [[for]{.mark} the [first]{.mark} time [in]{.mark} more than
**[twenty years]{.mark}**, there are [more]{.mark} closed
[**autocracies** than]{.mark} liberal
**[democracies]{.mark}**]{.underline} in the world. This shift includes
increased government repression of civil society organizations,
deteriorations in election quality, and increased government censorship
of media.

Although the features of political institutions in the United States
have not changed substantially in recent years, some political actors
have grown more willing to use these institutions to undermine
democratic practices. In the November 2022 elections in the United
States, election-denying candidates had modest success in statewide
races for governor, attorney general, and secretary of state. The
decentralized nature of U.S. election administration, however, means
that the actions of even a single state-level official could wreak
significant havoc on national electoral processes.

The high-profile losses in statewide races also conceal more ominous
signs at other levels. Counting statewide elected officials, candidates
for the House and Senate, and state legislative candidates, 226
election-denying [candidates
prevailed](https://www.brookings.edu/blog/fixgov/2022/11/15/democracy-on-the-ballot-hobbs-finishes-off-election-denial-for-now/),
or about 66 percent. Across [17
states](https://statesuniteddemocracy.org/resources/replacingtherefs/),
23 election-denying officials now hold positions that oversee voting and
electoral administration. Indeed, U.S. states can serve as laboratories
not necessarily for improved public policies and democratic practices,
but also for the [erosion of democratic
institutions](https://www.cambridge.org/core/journals/american-political-science-review/article/laboratories-of-democratic-backsliding/0742F08306EFDD8612539F089853E4FE?utm_source=SFMC&utm_medium=email&utm_content=Article&utm_campaign=New%20Cambridge%20Alert%20-%20Issues%20Society&WT.mc_id=New%20Cambridge%20Alert%20-%20Issues%20Society).
Moreover, many members of the majority party in the U.S. House of
Representatives are election deniers. The erosion of democratic
practices and norms therefore remains a serious threat in the United
States, especially as the 2024 elections approach.

[Such an [**erosion** is]{.mark} a [threat]{.mark} not only [to]{.mark}
our country's [**political** well-being]{.mark}, but [also]{.mark} to
**[economic]{.mark}** and financial [outcomes]{.mark}]{.underline}. [In
a
report](https://statesuniteddemocracy.org/resources/financial-and-economic-dangers-backsliding/) recently
­­­released by the Governance Program at the Brookings Institution and the
States United Democracy Center, I detail the [potential [**risks**
to]{.mark} institutional [**investors** of democratic
backsliding]{.mark} in the **U**]{.underline}nited
**[S]{.underline}**tates. Given such threats, [institutional investors
may have a **fiduciary** responsibility to take actions -- ranging from
undertaking **[analyses]{.mark}** more [attentive to political risk to
**reconsidering** investment holdings]{.mark} in certain states and
firms]{.underline}.

[My
report](https://statesuniteddemocracy.org/resources/financial-and-economic-dangers-backsliding/) draws
on a range of social scientific studies which examine the various
[**links** between]{.underline} the [nature of political
institutions]{.underline}, on the one hand, [and **economic** and
financial outcomes]{.underline}. These analyses [employ
[**quantitative** data]{.mark}]{.underline} from larger samples of

[[examin]{.mark}ing]{.underline} (for instance) the [**[higher

**non-democratic**]{.mark} political [systems]{.mark}]{.underline}.

Because of the size and unique position of the United States in the
global economy, such analyses often exclude the United States from their
datasets.  This choice usually makes sense from the point of view of
statistical analyses; but we should expect the dynamics relating
political risk to economic outcomes to be similar in the United States.
Indeed, [[investors]{.mark} in the **U**]{.underline}nited
**[S]{.underline}**tates may [stand to [lose]{.mark} more [from]{.mark}
democratic **[backsliding]{.mark}**]{.underline} than their counterparts
in other countries (see below). [While most large investors]{.underline}
have thus far **[assumed]{.underline}** that [the
**U**]{.underline}nited **[S]{.underline}**tates [is]{.underline}
somehow [exceptional -- insulating financial markets from the **risk**
of democratic backsliding -- this may well prove
**incorrect**]{.underline}.

What do we know about how the weakening of political institutions
affects economic outcomes? It is worth noting that the [material effects
of **[deteriorations]{.mark}** in governance [are far from limited
to]{.mark}]{.underline} countries experiencing [[**coups** and **civil
conflicts**]{.underline}]{.mark}. Rather, they can matter in more subtle
ways. In the report, I discuss three such channels. First, [[**weak**
rule of law]{.underline}]{.mark} -- that is, a de facto lack of respect,
often on the part of government officials, for de jure protections --
[**[undermines]{.mark}** longer-term [investment]{.mark}]{.underline}.
Around the world, investors prefer political systems in which the rule
of law is respected. When rule of law is weaker, firms worry more about
the security of their investments. Investment also is lower, all else
equal, when governments are less transparent in their provision of
information --- another hallmark of non-democratic political systems.

Certainly, multinational firms do invest in countries with weak
political institutions, because they sometimes find other features of
those economies sufficiently attract. But, when doing so, they tend to
make investments that are more easily reversible. In other instances,
they hope that investor-state dispute settlement mechanisms will address
their concerns -- although such mechanisms also depend on the
willingness of governments to respect legal processes.

Second, [[political uncertainty]{.underline}]{.mark} often
[[**disrupts** asset markets]{.underline}]{.mark}, such as those for
equities, currencies and bonds. [Elections and [government
**turnover**]{.mark} often [are sources of market
**uncertainty**]{.mark} and **volatility**]{.underline}. When investors
worry about whether an election will generate a radical shift in
government policies, or about whether election results will be
respected, decreases in stock market valuations, [**higher** interest
rate premiums and **currency crises** can follow]{.underline}.
Furthermore, the more democratic a country's national political
institutions are, the better able governments are to[ access sovereign
credit](https://www.cambridge.org/core/journals/british-journal-of-political-science/article/contingent-advantage-sovereign-borrowing-democratic-institutions-and-global-capital-cycles/68551C8C8B7FDFC3D0FE72C43AE9EEFC) markets.
The positive effect of democratic political institutions on access to
credit is especially pronounced when global financial conditions are
tighter, as they have been recently.

Third, the [[political actors who]{.mark} seek to [**undermine**
democratic practices]{.mark} often [have]{.mark}]{.underline} a
[[**populist** orientation]{.mark}. Populist]{.underline} political
[actors espouse **anti-elite** as well as]{.underline} strongly
[**nationalist** views, [and]{.mark}]{.underline} they tend to
**[[oppose]{.underline}]{.mark}** (and often actively seek to undermine)
[**international institutions** as well as [economic
**globalization**]{.mark}]{.underline}. As such, the electoral [success
of]{.underline} such [candidates would **erode** many]{.underline} of
the [gains]{.underline} that many firms, consumers and investors have
[experienced]{.underline} in the [post-World War II]{.underline} era.
While the multilateral institutions on which the post-World War II
liberal international order was based (such as the World Trade

[economy]{.mark}]{.underline}.

### IL \-- US Econ

#### The Panama Canal is strong and critical for the US.

Craig **Fuller 2024** CEO at FreightWaves, an organization that delivers
a complete and comprehensive view of the freight and logistics market,
'*Why the Panama Canal is so important to Trump*'
<https://www.freightwaves.com/news/why-the-panama-canal-is-so-important-to-trump>,
12/22/2024

"The [[Panama Canal is]{.mark} considered [a **VITAL** National Asset
for the U]{.mark}nited [S]{.mark}tates, [due to its **critical role in
America's Economy and National Security**]{.mark}]{.underline}," Trump
stated. 

The Panama [[Canal is **massively important for supply chains**]{.mark},
with [6% of global maritime trade]{.mark} passing through its
locks]{.underline}.

Approximately [[40% of U.S. container traffic utilizes the canal]{.mark}
annually]{.underline}, while the [[U.S. is]{.mark} also [the largest
user of the]{.mark} Panama [Canal]{.mark}. In 2021, [more than 73% of
all ships]{.mark} passing [through the canal]{.mark} [were heading to or
coming from U.S. ports]{.mark}.]{.underline} 

Initially opened in 1914 following a monumental construction effort led
by the United States, the canal was handed over to Panamanian control in
1999 under the Torrijos-Carter Treaties. These treaties, negotiated
during the Carter administration, have been a longstanding point of
contention among some U.S. politicians who see the transfer as a
strategic error.

[[This substantial percentage underscores the **canal's critical
function in reducing transit times**]{.underline}]{.mark} between the
Pacific and Atlantic Oceans, thereby [[enhancing the efficiency and
competitiveness of U.S. trade]{.underline}]{.mark}. This dependency
highlights why [[**any disruption or control shift could have profound
implications for the U.S**.]{.underline}]{.mark} economy, particularly
for industries reliant on timely and cost-effective shipping routes.

### 2ac -- Trump

#### Trump staying the course increases Chinese influence.

**Illueca '25** \[Alonso E.; 1/28/25; LLM in International and
Comparative Law from Columbia University, Research Associate Professor
at [Universidad Católica Santa María La
Antigua](https://www.linkedin.com/company/397692/); The Diplomat,
"Trump's Panama Gambit and the China Paradox,"
https://thediplomat.com/2025/01/trumps-panama-gambit-and-the-china-paradox/\]

For the past month, both before and after taking the oath of office on
January 20,  U.S. President Donald [[Trump]{.underline}]{.mark} has
[repeatedly **[threatened]{.mark}** the [territorial **integrity** of
Panama]{.mark}]{.underline}. More recently, during his inaugural
address, [he openly **pledged** to take back the Panama
Canal]{.underline}. In doing so, [he]{.underline} has [[**falsely**
claimed]{.underline}]{.mark} that [[China "operates" the canal]{.mark}
and]{.underline} that [Panama is]{.underline}, therefore, [in **breach**
of the]{.underline} canal's [neutrality regime]{.underline}. 

Such [[threats]{.underline}]{.mark} certainly
[[mean]{.underline}]{.mark} the [dawn of a **[new era]{.mark}** in
international politics, **[devolving]{.mark}** inter-state [relations
to]{.mark} a **["might makes right"]{.mark}** age]{.underline}. In such
a scenario, [Panama could face the same fate as the **Melians** at the
hands of the **Athenians**]{.underline}, as summed up in ancient Greek
historian Thucydides' famous axiom, [with the strong]{.underline} (the
**[U]{.underline}**nited **[S]{.underline}**tates) [doing what they can
and the weak (Panama) **suffering** what they must]{.underline}. 

Nonetheless, [Trump's bet is to not have to pursue **military**
conquest]{.underline} in the Athenian way. [He would rather have a
**complacent** Panama, accepting all U.S. demands]{.underline}. As shown
by the recent Colombia-U.S. [clash over
deportations](https://www.bbc.com/news/articles/c20p36e62gyo), [Trump's
approach seems to be **"cooperate or else."**]{.underline}

In the case of Panama, besides the claim of taking back the canal, it
could be argued that [[Trump]{.mark} is seeking to
**expel**]{.underline} any and all [Chinese influence from the country.
He seems to be [demanding **full submission**]{.mark} from
Panama]{.underline} by expelling the likes of CK Hutchison Holdings,
China Communications Construction Company, China Harbor Engineering
Company, Landbridge Group, and even Huawei from its jurisdiction. In the
U.S. Congress, some lawmakers have even suggested requiring Panama to
break diplomatic ties with Beijing and to reestablish them with Taipei.
[Such a **gambit** apparently seeks [to]{.mark} completely [**root out**
China's influence]{.mark} in Panama. However, this [may achieve]{.mark}
the **[opposite]{.mark}** of what is intended]{.underline}. 

The [[current]{.mark} U.S. [policy]{.mark} toward China's influence in
Panama [is **fundamentally flawed**]{.mark}]{.underline}. First, [it is
[**alienating** Panama]{.mark}]{.underline}, a treaty ally and a
partner, [which since receiving the canal has **willingly**
cooperated]{.underline} with the United States in most of its
international undertakings, including as part of the Coalition of the
Willing in the 2003 Iraq War, the Proliferation Security Initiative of
the same year and the Global Coalition to defeat ISIS established in
2014. Panama has even granted the U.S. permission to patrol Panamanian
sovereign waters. 

Second, [[the **U**]{.underline}]{.mark}nited
**[[S]{.underline}]{.mark}**tates has apparently [**[decoupled]{.mark}**
its [policy vis-à-vis Panama]{.mark} from]{.underline} the [defense of
common values and principles]{.underline}, particularly [[regarding
**democracy**, **transparency** and **human
rights**]{.underline}]{.mark}. 

Third, [[Trump]{.mark}'s approach **[ignores]{.mark}**]{.underline} the
[[efforts]{.mark} jointly undertaken by the U.S.]{.underline} (during
the Biden administration) [and Panama [to **counter** China's influence
by]{.mark}]{.underline} inter alia [[**halting** free trade]{.mark}
agreement talks]{.underline} [and]{.underline} the [projects in
Margarita Island]{.underline} (currently in arbitration) and Pilón Port,
[[which]{.underline}]{.mark} have [significantly **[reduced]{.mark}**
China's [influence]{.mark} around the canal]{.underline}. 

Fourth and last, [by [focusing **exclusively** on]{.mark} the
[**economic** and]{.mark} potential [**military** components]{.mark} of
China's presence in Panama]{.underline}, the [Trump]{.underline}
administration [[is **ignoring**]{.mark} other [realms where]{.mark}
China's [influence is]{.mark} more [**significant**. Here is]{.mark} the
[**paradox** tied to Trump]{.mark}'s gambit [as]{.mark}]{.underline} his
upcoming [[moves against Panama]{.mark} may]{.underline} well [[end up
increasing China's influence]{.underline}]{.mark} in the country. 

The [2024 China Index](https://china-index.io/), which measures China's
influence in 98 jurisdictions,
ranks [Panama](https://china-index.io/country/Panama) 12th globally and
second regionally (Latin America), which means that it is among the
countries where Beijing exerts a relatively high level of influence.
When analyzed, however, the data compiled demonstrates that it is in
Panama's economic and military domains where China exerts less influence
and seems fairly contained. In fact, China's influence in Panama is more
significant in the fields of media (59.1 percent), academia (47.7
percent), society (68.2 percent), law enforcement (50 percent),
technology (40 percent), domestic politics (82.5 percent), and foreign
policy (68.2 percent), which sharply contrast with China's limited
influence in Panama's economy (27.3 percent) and military (30 percent). 

[By focusing on]{.underline} the [[**immediate**
returns]{.underline}]{.mark} -- i.e. forcing Panama to expel CK
Hutchison Holdings and others -- [Trump is implementing]{.underline} a
[[policy]{.mark} based [on economic coercion]{.mark} **against** an ally
without]{.underline} taking due regard for the facts [regarding China's
influence]{.underline} in the country. [In the short term this may
[**align** Panama]{.mark} even [further with China]{.mark}
in]{.underline} the realms of [**domestic** politics and **foreign**
policy, which in the medium and long terms could be [accompanied
by]{.mark}]{.underline} a [[**consolidation** of]{.mark} China's
[influence]{.mark} in the country [in]{.mark}]{.underline} the realms of
[[**media**, **society**, **academia**, and **law
enforcement**]{.underline}]{.mark}. 

Since the establishment of diplomatic ties in 2017, it is undeniable
that China has exponentially increased its influence in Panama. This is
in large measure due to the United States' incremental passivity in its
bilateral relations with Panama. For example, during almost five of
these seven-plus years of China-Panama bilateral relations the United
States did not have an ambassador in Panama, while China had two
different ambassadors uninterruptedly advancing its agenda. 

In order to counter China's influence in Panama, the United States needs
a comprehensive and values-oriented approach. It is precisely through
the support of democracy, transparency, and human rights that
substantive improvements can be introduced, and foreign interference be
prevented. [If the [current trend]{.mark} **continues** it would further
**[empower]{.mark}**]{.underline} autocrats like China's
[[Xi]{.underline}]{.mark} Jinping and Russia's Vladimir Putin. In the
end, [it is very likely that **[economic coercion]{.mark}** or
even]{.underline} the [threat of it would **force** Panama to
"cooperate" -- but]{.underline} make no mistake, [such a policy based on
**threats**, **devoid** of principles and values, which **ignores**
past-joint efforts and **lacks** a comprehensive and factual approach,
may well [end up having]{.mark} the [**paradoxical** effect of
**increasing** China's influence]{.mark} in Panama]{.underline}. 

### 2ac -- alt pathways

#### Alternate routes to Panama inevitable -- climate change and infrastructure concerns limit shipping traffic

**Hogan '25** \[Dennis M.; 1/1/25; Professor in the History and
Literature program at Harvard; The New York Times, "The Panama Canal Has
a Big Problem, but It's Not China or Trump,"
https://www.nytimes.com/2025/01/01/opinion/panama-canal-trump-china-drought.html\]

In 2023, on a research trip to Panama, I booked a day tour of the Panama
Canal. I expected to hear the usual story about the canal's epic
construction, importance in world trade and successful expansion to
allow for larger modern ships. What I did not expect was the
[**[overwhelming]{.mark}** sense of **concern**, even
**[panic]{.mark}**, among people who depend on the canal]{.underline}
for their livelihoods.

It was July, the middle of Panama's rainy season. But the rains had been
sparse, and [[water levels]{.mark} in the canal]{.underline} had
[[sunk]{.mark} to **troubling lows**]{.underline}. Without freshwater
from rain, our guide explained, the locks on the canal could not
operate.

I remembered that visit after President-elect Donald [[Trump
said]{.underline}]{.mark} recently that [the [Chinese were
**threatening**]{.mark} America's interests]{.underline} at the canal,
[and]{.underline} he [engaged in some saber rattling]{.underline} by
[suggesting]{.underline} that [the **U**]{.underline}nited
**[S]{.underline}**tates [could take back **control** of the
passage]{.underline}, which was returned to Panama exactly 25 years ago
Tuesday. The handover treaties were a signature achievement of President
Jimmy Carter, who died on Sunday; Mr. Trump's comments were in keeping
with longstanding criticism that the move was a strategic mistake.

But Mr. [[Trump misunderstands]{.underline}]{.mark} the [[**true
threat** to]{.mark} U.S. commerce through [Panama]{.mark}. If the goal
is securing affordable access]{.underline} to the transit point over the
[long term, [it is **climate change**, not Chinese
influence]{.mark}]{.underline}, that [U.S. [policymakers should
**worry** about]{.mark}]{.underline}.

Here's why. [Sending [a single ship]{.mark} through]{.underline} the
canal's locks [can [use]{.mark} around [**50 million** gallons of
water]{.mark}]{.underline}, mainly freshwater collected from Lake Gatún.
[Though the canal is, for the moment, operating at **full** capacity, a
[**drier** climate and]{.mark} greater [**demand** for]{.mark} drinking
[water]{.mark} have]{.underline} in recent years [reduced]{.underline}
the [**volume** of available water. That]{.underline}
has [[[forced]{.underline}]{.mark} the state-run
[[Panama]{.underline}]{.mark} Canal
Authority](https://www.nytimes.com/2024/08/14/business/panama-canal-drought.html) at
times [[to **limit**]{.underline}]{.mark} the [number of daily
[passages]{.mark}]{.underline} through the canal, at one point
[[by]{.mark} as much as **[40 percent]{.mark}**]{.underline}.

[With **less rain**]{.underline}, the [reservoirs fill up more
**slowly**, which means less water available]{.underline} to operate the
locks, [which means **fewer ships**]{.underline} can [pass]{.underline}.
Hence, the 2023-24 [[drought]{.underline}]{.mark}, among the worst on
record, [[**slowed** transits]{.mark} and [drove up]{.mark} transit
[**prices**, causing long **delays**]{.mark}, [more **expensive**
consumer
goods](https://unctad.org/publication/review-maritime-transport-2024) [and]{.mark}]{.underline}
greater [**[instability]{.mark}** in shipping routes]{.underline}. These
were probably the increases Mr. Trump referred to as a "rip-off."

The [limited]{.underline} number of [passages]{.underline} has [led
to [**auctions**](https://www.lloydslist.com/LL1151913/Trump-to-Panama-Reduce-ridiculous-tolls-or-US-will-take-back-canal#:~:text=Panama%20suffered%20a%20historic%20drought,year%2C%20which%20ended%20in%20September.) for
passage rights that]{.underline} further **[inflated]{.underline}** the
[growing cost of shipping]{.underline} goods [through the
canal]{.underline}. (The canal authority [increased
tolls](https://www.nytimes.com/2024/03/21/business/panama-canal-water-revenue.html) just
before the 2023 drought began.) In the short term, [reduced access
causes goods to take **longer**]{.underline} to reach their
destinations, [and]{.underline} they [**cost** more]{.underline} when
they arrive. Over the medium term, [[companies]{.mark} have **begun** to
[seek [**alternate**
routes](https://www.economist.com/the-americas/2024/01/09/the-dwindling-of-the-panama-canal-boosts-rival-trade-routes)]{.mark}]{.underline} and
different methods [of moving goods]{.underline}. Some [projects,
[like]{.mark} a [**[railway]{.mark}**
corridor](https://www.wsj.com/video/series/breaking-ground/mexico-is-building-a-75-billion-trade-route-to-compete-with-panama-canal/B9342697-6F40-43EE-84A3-945F6EF914E0) [across
**southern Mexico**]{.mark}]{.underline}, have [emerged [to **directly
compete**]{.mark} with the]{.underline} Panama [Canal]{.underline}. Over
the long term, [as trade **volume** and [ship **sizes**
increase](https://www.nytimes.com/2021/03/30/business/economy/container-ships-suez-canal.html) while]{.underline}
the amount of available [water decreases, [the canal
could]{.mark}]{.underline} well [**[lose]{.mark}** market [share,
**declining** in]{.mark}]{.underline} both [**usefulness** and strategic
**[importance]{.mark}**]{.underline}.

The 2023-24 drought was due in part to a [strong El Niño
effect](https://wmo.int/news/media-centre/el-nino-weakens-impacts-continue),
as rising surface temperatures in the Pacific Ocean altered weather
patterns worldwide. Scientists generally agree that climate change is
making El Niño events [more
frequent](https://e360.yale.edu/digest/el-nino-climate-change) and [more
severe](https://www.seattletimes.com/business/saving-the-panama-canal-will-take-years-and-cost-billions-if-its-even-possible/#:~:text=World-,Saving%20the%20Panama%20Canal%20will%20take%20years,billions%2C%20if%20it's%20even%20possible&text=The%20vestiges%20of%20an%20ancient,stumps%20rise%20above%20the%20waterline.).
Higher temperatures have [increased the
evaporation](https://www.wired.com/story/panama-canal-climate-change/) of
water off the reservoir, too, further reducing the water supply.

Mr. [Trump's **insinuations** about Chinese influence]{.underline}, his
demands for lower transit fees and his talk of returning the canal to
U.S. control have, of course, [not won him **admiration** in
Panama]{.underline}, least of all among civic leaders more concerned
with solving the canal's challenges.

In recent days, President José Raúl Mulino of Panama has repeatedly
rejected accusations of Chinese influence.
He [declared](https://x.com/France24_en/status/1872696809811808635) at a
news conference last week that "the Panama Canal is Panamanian and
belongs to Panamanians" and that Panamanian control of the waterway had
cost the country "blood, sweat and tears." There is [no legal
mechanism](https://apnews.com/article/trump-panama-canal-roosevelt-treaties-1999-handover-8db28fd0249377a3f102cbb0a81e9356) by
which Mr. Trump might demand its return.

Panamanians are rightly proud of their administration of the canal over
the past 25 years; in suggesting that the United States retains any
claim to it, Mr. Trump risks alienating a country that [remains
friendly](https://www.state.gov/u-s-relations-with-panama/) to the
United States despite a long history of U.S. arrogance toward the
Panamanian people.

Panama has been a global crossroads since the 19th century. A sea route
across the isthmus was [first
imagined](https://pancanal.com/en/early-plans/) by the Spanish in the
16th century; the overland route between the oceans rose to prominence
in the 1840s and 1850s during the [California Gold
Rush](https://muse.jhu.edu/pub/255/monograph/book/68253%5C). Through the
19th century, the United States competed against other imperial powers,
mainly Britain and France, for land and influence in Central America.
Controlling Panama was a [key
feature](https://www.pbs.org/newshour/world/panama-canal-helped-make-u-s-world-power) of
the American rise to hemispheric dominance and overseas empire in the
wake of the Spanish-American War of 1898.

Panamanians have benefited from that competition, but they have equally
been victimized by it: Panama became independent in 1903,
but [sovereignty came at the
cost](https://history.state.gov/milestones/1899-1913/panama-canal) of
ceding the Canal Zone to the United States. Both before and after the
construction of the canal, U.S. military intervention was a constant
threat. Since the 1840s, the United States has [intervened
frequently](https://revista.drclas.harvard.edu/united-states-interventions/) to
protect its commercial interests in Panama. U.S. troops deployed to
Panama 13 times from 1856 to 1903. Most seriously, [an invasion in
1989](https://apnews.com/article/62c740ff27059d3b1579f71a1348f018) resulted
in the deaths of hundreds of Panamanian civilians; unofficial estimates
put the number in the thousands.

The history of U.S.-Panama relations is neither simple nor
straightforward. The United States built the canal, and without support
from the U.S. military, Panama probably would not have been able to
secede from Colombia and establish itself as an independent nation. The
United States has served as an occupier but also a major trading
partner, ally and source of cultural influence.

Many Americans have [Panamanian
roots](https://www.pewresearch.org/race-and-ethnicity/fact-sheet/us-hispanics-facts-on-panamanian-origin-latinos/#:~:text=Panamanians%20are%20the%2015th%2Dlargest,growing%20from%20100%2C000%20to%20240%2C000.),
and an increasing number of [Americans
live](https://www.forbes.com/sites/kathleenpeddicord/2024/11/22/guide-to-retiring-in-panama/) in
Panama. Now migrants from places such as Haiti and Venezuela [are
crossing](https://www.cfr.org/article/crossing-darien-gap-migrants-risk-death-journey-us) the
Daríén Gap straddling Colombia and Panama en route to the United States,
prompting a fresh humanitarian emergency and new challenges for both
Panama and the United States. These are nations whose fates are tied
together.

From Panama to Greenland, Mr. Trump's expansionist ambitions point to a
larger truth: In a world increasingly dependent on extended supply
chains that crisscross the globe, mastery of logistics translates to
economic security. Taking Panama (as Teddy Roosevelt did), Mr. Trump
imagines, might secure U.S. access to the canal, and buying Greenland
(as he has also suggested) would guarantee a [U.S.
presence](https://www.nytimes.com/2024/12/27/opinion/greenland-trump-real-estate.html) at
the entrance to a future northwest passage through the melting Arctic
ice. In Mr. Trump's view of international competition, it seems sea
routes are to be seized, controlled and monopolized, not shared among
nations with equal access to all, [as the Panama Canal
is](https://pancanal.com/wp-content/uploads/2022/07/neutrality-treaty.pdf).

[Costs of **transiting** the canal are going up]{.underline}. But
[[neither]{.underline}]{.mark} so-called [[Panamanian greed nor Chinese
influence **determines**]{.mark} these [costs. The truth
is]{.mark}]{.underline} more **[[straightforward]{.underline}]{.mark}**,
if more daunting: [[If we want]{.mark} to ensure fair, equal and
[**sustainable** access]{.mark} to the canal, [it is **climate change**
we'll]{.mark} have to [beat, not the Chinese]{.mark}]{.underline}.

#### Climate change ensures alternate shipping lanes

Narjiss **Ghajour 24**, Engineer and writer for MARPRO marine

[waterways]{.mark}]{.underline}. A prominent [[example]{.mark}
[is]{.mark} the [Panama Canal]{.mark}, [which]{.mark} has
[faced]{.mark}]{.underline} transit [[restrictions]{.mark} due to a

industry to adapt its strategies and consider both the impacts of
climate change and measures to reduce its own environmental footprint.

#### New Arctic shipping routes threaten the Panama Canal

Adrian **Piecyk**, 20**21**. The Arctic Institute. [A Bridge Across Two
Oceans: The Arctic Challenge to Panama Canal Shipping \| The Arctic
Institute -- Center for Circumpolar Security
Studies](https://www.thearcticinstitute.org/bridge-across-two-oceans-arctic-challenge-panama-canal-shipping/)

If Given a pair of darts and a map, you would be hard-pressed to hit two
spots more visibly different than Panama and the Arctic Ocean[. These
two places, disparate though they may be, are being brought together by
the [global threat of climate change]{.mark}. The [disappearance
of]{.mark} the [Arctic]{.mark} Ocean's summer [ice]{.mark} over the
course of the next century could also [herald]{.mark} the [disappearance
of]{.mark} Panama's greatest economic engine -- the [Panama
Canal]{.mark} -- [as shipping]{.mark} is instead [redirected through
shorter]{.mark} Arctic [routes]{.mark}]{.underline}. The scale of this
threat prompts three questions: what advantages does Arctic shipping
provide over the Panama Canal, how is Panama confronting the issue, and
how realistic is the prospect of greater volumes of Arctic shipping? To
answer these questions, this article will first contrast the merits and
importance of both Arctic and Panama Canal shipping, followed by an
examination of Panamanian news articles and government statements, and
then conclude with a feasibility analysis and future projections. The
picture which emerges is a mixed one: Panama's media tends to focus on
more immediate threats than melting Arctic ice, and while the expansion
of Arctic shipping itself seems inevitable, it remains to be seen how
quickly and how practically it could overshadow the Canal.

The Lifeline of Panama

From as early as 12,000 BC, the Indigenous ancestors of the modern
Ngöbe, Kuna, and Buglere peoples inhabited the Atlantic and Pacific
coasts of the thin isthmus that later became Panama.^1)^ This gap
between the world's two largest oceans was only bridged in 1914, with
the completion of the Panama Canal. Since assuming control of the Canal
under the auspices of the Panama Canal Authority (ACP) in 1999, it has
formed the cornerstone of Panama's economic and diplomatic efforts. The
Canal and its associated services encompass approximately 40% of
Panama's economy,^2)^ and as of writing, revenues from the Canal have
essentially increased five-fold since 1999. Official figures for 2019
set a new record, with tariffs on 468,779,276 PC/UMS^3)^ net tons
generating \$2.592 billion USD, despite a dip in total transits from the
preceding year.^4)^ Growth has been brisk since the completion of a
long-awaited modernization in 2016, an achievement touted by the ACP as
"re-inaugurating" the Canal's role as a global trade hub. The
modernization project, which focused on the expansion of the Canal
locks, has already achieved demonstrable success thanks to the expanded
locks now accommodating up to 90% of the world's crude oil and natural
gas tankers.^5)^ With rising revenues and new technical feats, how could
the melting of polar ice drastically affect even a modernized Panama
Canal?

The Arctic Passages

The Panama Canal's success depends on its convenience -- shorter
distances equal lower shipping costs. The Canal revolutionized
international trade upon its opening, as it allowed vessels to sail
directly between the Atlantic and Pacific Oceans. [What happens, then,
when an even [more convenient route opens]{.mark}, and seemingly [with
no upfront cost]{.mark}? Just as the Panama Canal earns record revenues
year-after-year, so too is the Arctic Ocean seeing record rates of
melting ice]{.underline}. As of August 2020, the Arctic Ocean maintains
approximately 5.08 million km^2 ^of thinner, late summer ice coverage,
but it has lost 3.15 million km^2 ^of late summer ice since 1979, at a
rate of close to 76,800km^2^ per year.^6)^ [[The Northwest
Passage]{.mark}, a sea route which winds its way along Canada's Arctic
coast and provides the shortest route to the east coast of North America
from East Asia, could be [ice-free and navigable in summer]{.mark}
within a matter of decades]{.underline}. Its sister, the Northeast
Passage, chiefly follows the northern Russian coast, and so provides an
alternative to shipping through the Suez. Between these two lies the
more difficult but direct Transpolar Route, which could circumvent many
of the Northwest Passage's navigational, depth, and territorial issues
were its summer ice to disappear, as could happen by 2050, according to
scientific predictions.^7)^ The extent of thick, multi-year ice is down
60 percent from 1980, and its replacement by thinner, seasonal ice could
preclude the need for ice breakers for Arctic shipping.^8)^ A shorter,
toll-free transit route through the Arctic means not only less cost for
freight, but also reduced carbon emissions, and these factors could
seriously affect the decision-making of Panama's key transit partners.

Arctic Voyages -- Fluke or Trend?

Over the past decade, several high-profile transits through the
Northwest Passage specifically have highlighted the danger of this
alternate route to the Panama Canal's dominance. Travelling from
Vancouver to Finland in 2013, the carrier *Nordic Orion *saved \$200,000
and four days of sailing by taking the Northwest Passage instead of the
Panama Canal.^9)^ More recently, the Finnish icebreaker *Nordica *set a
record in 2017 by travelling 10,000 km in a mere 24 days by a similar
route.^10)^ Though these transits by individual ships have a limited
impact on a broader scale, they nevertheless set a precedent which could
herald far greater volumes of traffic through the Arctic in the near
future.

A Looming Economic Disaster

Considering that the [[Northwest Passage]{.mark} would [best facilitate
shipping between]{.mark} the [US]{.mark} East Coast [and]{.mark}
[Asia]{.mark}, the [picture]{.mark} becomes even [grimmer for
Panama]{.mark}]{.underline}. As of 2019, official stats record that a
staggering 39 percent of total tonnage passing through the Panama Canal
travels along the East Coast-Asia route, translating to 180,564,000
PC/UMS net tons out of 468,779,000 total.^11)^ The next largest route by
cargo throughput, between the US East Coast and the west coast of South
America, comes out to 38,640,000 PC/UMS, or a mere 8 percent of total
tonnage. The United States alone accounts for no less than 66.4 percent
of Canal traffic by origin and destination, followed perhaps
unsurprisingly by China at 13.6 percent.^12)^ On a global scale, the
[Panama Canal facilitates approximately 5 percent of total oceanic
shipping, an impressive figure,]{.underline} but one which is
overwhelmingly dependent on the flow of trade between the US and China.

[Given the inexorable march of climate change and unprecedented ice melt
in the North, the **question** facing the Panama Canal in terms of
future traffic loss is less "if" and **more "how much**."]{.underline}
The East Coast -- Asia route is indisputably the cornerstone of Panama
Canal traffic, and if [the US and China leap at the opportunity for up
to [30 percent shorter journeys compared to]{.mark} taking [the
Canal]{.mark}]{.underline},^13)^ the losses in revenue for Panama could
be staggering. Using the 2019 figures as a baseline, a conservative loss
of 25 percent along the East Coast -- Asia route alone would see PC/UMS
tonnage drop by 45,141,000, or more than 7,000,000 PC/UMS than the next
leading route has total. A far worse [scenario with 75 percent losses
would see a staggering drop of 135,423,000 PC/UMS, a whopping 29 percent
of total tonnage]{.underline}.

Tariffs would likewise have to be adjusted to maintain competitiveness
with Arctic alternatives. Though the fees levied depend on a wide range
of circumstances, the largest container ships of 10,000 TEU (twenty-foot
equivalent containers) and over could expect to pay upwards of
\$1,000,000 USD, while older Panamax vessels average to around
\$500,000.^14)^ As such, [Panama would not only be receiving
substantially less shipping through the Canal, but even the shipping it
receives would be paying less. Given the potential scale of the threat
facing Panama if Arctic ice continues to melt at present rates, one
would imagine that it would be front-and-centre in national
discourse.]{.underline}
